“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY. N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy .
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Comolete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. *
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
| an d has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the ela’ms of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aerainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, Tub
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red coveris equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far,North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Business
The Caligraph Writing Machine .
iff!
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
T.he Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

Dear Bulletin,— Nettleship, the Glad-
stonian oculist, is not a Maorilander. He and his
brothers are natives of Kettering, Northampton-
shire.—Yours, J.P.
State-school teacher registering child for
ad mission : ‘ What is your father’s name ?”
Smith.” “ Christian name?” “John.” “What
work does he do ?” “ Stoker.” “ Where does he
live? “Please, he’s dead.”
Selector: “ Are you looking for a job?”
Tired Swaginan (whose air betokens other circum-
stances): “Am I looking for a job ! In the name
oi God, man, you cannot possibly suppose for one
instant that I am carrying this parcel (kick-
ing swag) about this country for pleasuee !”
Among a heap of obsolete royal fictions
lies the pretence that a Queen’s Drawing-Room is
open to any respectable woman suffering from a
desire to crouch before her Sovereign. At some
remote period it was vaguely supposed and hazily
alleged that a monarch on certain days was
prepared to receive all sorts and conditions of
visitors under fairly easy regulations. No official
announcement has ever been made to the contrary,
because average respectable subjects might object
to hearing that they are unfit to enter the royal
presence. _ The Sovereign has simply bluffed ’em
off in an indirect manner by imposing abnormal
toilette expenses upon the devotees, and hiding
the original avowed purpose of the function behind
a blaze of diamonds. One of these days a strong-
minded matron, a clean, tidy, virtuous mother of
nine, bearing a certificate of respectability from
the parson, may demand admission to a Drawing-
Room. The Lord Chamberlain, or more likely a
policeman, will repel this honest loyalist, and
perhaps she will hold a public meeting, and—well,
revolutions have started from less tangible pro-
vocation.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large qnan-
titles of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Dear Bulletin, — A. purchased a horse
from B. Cash paid; receipt given. The horse
had been turned loose on a neighbouring run some
months previously (run the property of a J.P.)
A. bought the animal knowing he would have to
find and secure him. Searches unsuccessfully
twioe._ Squatter’s manager offered to run the
horse in for 10s. A. accepts offer. Horse run in.
Squatter refuses to allow removal, demanding .£3
for agistment. No arrangement had been made
between squatter and B. for any charge. A. seeks
advice from sergeant of police, who recommends
him to go to station and demand the horse. A.
does as directed. Squatter again refuses to give
up possession. Sergeant of police next gives A.
letter to squatter, directing him to deliver horse.
Squatter declines. A. seeks advice from the war-
den, and is told to summon squatter for the
animal. Does so. Case heard by warden, who
decides that A. must pay £3 demanded bv
squatter. Money paid ; horse delivered. War-
den then recommends A. to summon B. for the
agistment charge of £3. A. acts as advised. Case
heard again by warden, who dismisses summons
witnout costs, on ground that A. had no authority
from B. to pay the £3. Warden fully aware of
this at time he advises A. to summon B.—Yours,
Granite. [A hard case ; but A. should have pro-
tected himself before buying a pig in a bag. If
facts as stated, the warden’s conduct does him no
credit.—Ed. B.]

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bool making is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tun Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which yon may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maoleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But ve desire those sendiny MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the -preservation or
transmission of such MSS.
LONDON.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal :— The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also: It. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queens-
land and Soutb Australia.
jn consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £l, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage.
No papers will be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this rule.
Notice to Subscribers.
Coloured wrapper denotes expiration of subscription.

Notice to Agents.
MAORILAND, TASMANIA and WEST
AUSTRALIA
Owing to Governments of the above-named
provinces having imposed Half-penny postage on
all papers from Ist July, 1894, agents are in-
formed from that date the wholesale rate for
The Bulletin will be altered from Four Shillings
to Four Shillings and Sixpence per dozen.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 11. 1894.
Treasurer Carter’s Tariff.
Victoria has closed its financial year with
another ghastly deficit of £665,000, and its
accumulated shortage now amounts to nearly
£2,000,000. The optimistic wine and spirit
merchant, whom Premier Patterson turned
into a Minister of Finance, decided last
year that the depression had reached its
lowest ebb, and framed his budget on the
assumption that a distinct improvement
might* shortly he expected. But the im-
provement never came, and instead the
depression grew darker and more doleful.
The revenue fell some £844,000 below
the estimate, and but for the frantic
efforts at economy, which were made dur-
ing the latter half of the year, the deficit
for the twelve mouths would have been
well on towards a million. The public
expenditure is now down about £2,200,000,
and the revenue £3,000,000 below the figures
of Gillies’ stupendous boom budget, but
while the expenditure seems to have reached
almost the lowest possible limit the revenue
keeps falling. No one, either, has any
reasonable theory as to where or when it is
going to stop. It has now got hack to about
the level of nine or ten years ago, in the
peaceful days before the boom madness came
upon the land, and even if it remains at
that figure it is difficult to know what is
going to happen. In those old days the
amount now available was more than enough
for all purposes, and under any ordinary cir-
cumstances it would he enough to-day. But
the circumstances are not ordinary. During
the last decade the Coalition Ministry
covered the country with little cockspur
railways which barely meet the wages of
the man who scares the goats off the line,
and made frightful additions to the public
debt to pay for them ; and it made billets in
the public service for its relatives and
friends, and their friends, relatives, and
remote acquaintances, who cannot now be
got rid of without payment of heavy com-
pensation ; and the land was dotted all over
with gaols and docks and breakwaters and
irrigation works and reclamation works ; and
120,000 immigrants were attracted into the
country within five years, over and above the
natural increase of population ; and taxation
was reduced ; and subsidies were granted in
all directions ; and everything was swollen
to a state of bloated and dropsical grandeur.
• # *
Then, when the inevitable fall came to
pass, a succession of weak-kneed Ministers
came and feebly pottered among the ruins.
Munro declined to make any serious effort
at additional taxation on the ground that
everything would presently come right; and
carried his big shortage forward. Graham
Berry came and did likewise. Carter
followed and also did likewise. But with
four huge successive deficiencies actually
accomplished, and a fifth one in prospective,
the Patterson Government has at last
realised the necessity of taking some more
than usually v gorous step. It has so far
proved itself a pitiable failure of a Govern-
ment, with no policy, no power of initiative,
and no capacity for doing anything great in
an emergency. Also, its members, indi-
vidually and collectively, are of the most
incurably Tory order, and have a deep and
holy reverence for capital in every form.
The land-tax, in Yictoria, is a miserable
device which produces barely £125,000 per
annum, but though various suggestions have
been made for the increase of this feeble
little impost, and for a more vigorous system
of collection, nothing has been done, or
seems likely to be done. The idea of levying
income or property taxes, after the plan
adopted in Maoriland, is about equally
abhorrent to the Patterson Cabinet, al-
though £1,000,000 per annum might easily
be obtained from this source. The dead
capitalist is almost as sacred in its estimation
as the live one, and various suggestions about
materially increasing the legacy and succes-
sion duties have been received, and dropped
witli a shudder. And when it was recently
suggested, in connection with the proposed
Credit Foncier scheme, that the Government
should issue, and lend out on mortgage,
£5,000,000 in State notes —a hazardous but
by no means hopeless expedient, and one
which could not possibly make the circum-
stances of the province any more desperate
than they already are—the dull, stupid
financiers of the Ministry received it with
horror.
« * »
To a Cabinet which dare not tax wealth in
any shape—which cannot meet its liabilities
and dare not repudiate—which dare not do
anything new and is too miserably stereo-
typed to know how to do it in any case—
which has practically no public lands to sell,
and consequently cannot resort to that
blessed expedient which helped so materially
to keep New South Wales afloat through
many years of Foreign-trade administration
—there is nothing left but to make one more
endeavour to screw revenue out of the poor.
And to this end Treasurer Carter has issued
a revised tariff which has been hailed by the
Foreign-trade organs of Sydney as the first
great step towards a Freetrade upheaval in
Yictoria, Except for the fact that Treasurer
Carter, being an importer and, consequently,
a Freetrader within his own narrow limits,
would gladly abolish these protective duties
which interfere with “business,” there is
no visible reason for this jubilation. The
new tariff is only a mean and mongrel ini-
quity, which reduces a few old duties,
abolishes a few more, and piles on a vast
number of new ones, avowedly for revenue
purposes. The material reductions are
mainly those upon luxuries consumed largely
by the rich, and the increases are mainly
upon those articles, which are used equally
by rich and poor. About the biggest reduc-
tion of all is in the duties upon champagne
and other imported wines. The duties on
imported brandy, whisky, and all other
kinds of spirits, are considerably diminished ;
and so are the excise imposts on the blue
ruin of local manufacture. This fatherly
yearning on the part of grog-merchant
Carter after the interests of the whisky
trade is eminently touching. The duties bn
imported beer are cut down ; likewise, to
a small extent, those on sundry articles
of clothing and machinery. And, on the
other hand, a considerable number of articles
which have hitherto passed free are to be
squashed by a ten per cent. duty. Many of
these are goods which cannot be manu-
factured locally, therefore the duty will only
have the effect of raising prices and pro-
ducing revenue, and is just as statesmanlike
a device as a poll-tax, or a charge of so much
per head for the right to remain alive.
* • *
Why this feeble and formless tariff should
be hailed as an instalment of Freetrade
passes the comprehension of man. Almost
the only definite feature about it is that it is
calculated to produce £167,000 per annum
more than its predecessor ; therefore, if it is
an instalment of the blessed policy of Free-
trade, and costs that amount more than did
Protection, there is room for awful fore-
boding as to what Freetrade will cost when
the whole of it arrives. But, in reality, there
is no question of any fiscal policy whatever
in the matter. The tariff which Carter has
evolved out of his eminently solemn and
respectable inner consciousness is merely
a heartless money-making device, con-
structed with a soulful regard for the
liquor connection, and with profound and
earnest consideration of the interests of Pro-
perty. For Victoria must either make
further retrenchments on an enormous scale
—which seems practically impossible con-
sidering how much has been already done in
that direction—or repudiate part, at least, of
its liabilities ; or else it must raise more
revenue. It is cursed with an elective Upper
House, based on a purely property qualifica-
tion, which cannot be “ swamped,” or made
amenable to the popular will by any lawful
means, and which objects strongly to the
taxation of wealth in any shape. It is
further cursed with a Lower House which is
filled with bitterly conservative bankers and
lawyers, and spirit-merchants and land-
boomers, and all manner of more or less
shady financiers. It has no Democratic
party worthy of the name, and no Democratic
press, and no vigorous public opinion to
exercise pressure on its dull and retrogressive
legislators; and since Higinbothamvanished
from politics and Berry flickered out and
became a mere extinct volcano, it has been
given over to a long series of dreary medio-
crities, each mediocrity being, if possible, a
shade more dreary than the one who went
before him. But the first beginning and prime
cause of all its troubles is its elective Coun-
cil, which has given to a mean little pluto-
cracy the power to block every Democratic
movement, and has entrenched it so strongly
that it can only be shifted by its own act, or
by revolution. Under the pressure of this
irremoveable incubus Victoria has travelled
steadily downward until it has struck the
lowest depth up to date in Carter, the wine
importer, and James Brown Patterson, the
butcher of Castlemaine. And if anything
lower lies beyond them, the outlook is melan-
choly beyond all power of expression.
Our Undemocratic Democracy.
We are accustomed to speak of the Aus-
tralian provinces as democracies, of their
systems of government as democratic systems
of government. In a sense, this is true ; in
the best sense, it is untrue. That is a demo-
cracy where every citizen has a voice in the
government. But in New South Wales, for
example, 260,200 citizens over 21 years of
age have no vote because they are women.
Of 352,000 males over 21, excluding abor-
igines, 30,260 are, taking the figures as
at Ist January, ’94, disabled by law—
viz., Chinese, 12,200 ; other aliens, 20,400 ;
lunatics, 1834 ; State-aided paupers, 2103;
convicts in gaol, 2069 ; fully-paid naval and
military, 957 ; police magistrates, 76 ; police,
1621. 23,685 have no vote because their
names are not on the electoral rolls, and
possibly at the recent election 5000 were dis-
franchised through change of residence.
The available voting strength was thus re-
duced to 293,055, of whom 89,513 failed to
vote, leaving 203,542 voters to represent
581,490 adult persons. But all the votes
cast for defeated candidates are wasted—
that is, the wishes of 105,999 voters are
disregarded, and only the preferences of
97,543 —47 per cent, of actual voters, 30 per
cent, of eligible adult males, 16 per cent, of
adult persons—are represented in Parlia-
ment.
• • ♦ *
Now, the rule of the majority is not
Democratic Government. “ Government of
the people, by the people, for the people ”
implies the equal representation of the
people in the governing body. In the last
resort, the decision of the majority must be
final, but till then the minority are entitled
to a proportional voice and vote. The pre-
sent system of ascertaining the wishes of
the people, even when perfectly applied,
deliberately silences the minority—who are
often only less considerable than the majority
—in the first stage of representation. A
Parliament elected by 100,000 voters may
act in direct opposition to the wishes of
90,000 —who have individually an equal right
and competence to record their wishes. But
this is not all. A question comes up for
decision in Parliament which divides the
representatives of the governing majority.
Possibly the representatives of 56,000 voters
may decide it without reference to the
wishes of 135,000, who, most likely, oppose
the decision —since 90,000 voted in the first
instance against the majority representatives,
and 45,000 for those who oppose the Parlia-
mentary majority.
• * •
The case of New South Wales is even
worse. Passing the question of woman
suffrage ; in any constituency 2574 males
are, on an average, qualified to be electors.
The average number actually on the rolls is
2344. The average number who voted is
1628. The average number who elected a
representative in Parliament is 780, The
new Parliament represents a minority of
voters, a minority of electors, and an im-
mensely smaller minority of adult men.
This is not representative government; and
it is only a bastard form of democratic
government. An attempt has been made to
classify the votes given to defeated candi-
dates, and to show that the wishes of the
voters were, to some extent, suited by the
success of other candidates with similar
general views. But such reasoning is fruit-
less and inconclusive. Tariff cries doubtless
exercised an influence ; but a multitude of
other causes co-operated to control every
vote. The plain fact remains that, on the
average, in every one of the 125 constitu-
encies, the man whom 780 voters wanted in
Parliament was returned, although 848
voters did not want him, but somebody else.
• • •
Under such a system there is no guarantee
whatever that measures adopted by Parlia-
ment represent the wish of the people. The
chances are they do not, since a majority of
the people have voted against those by whom
the measures will be carried. Only the
adoption of some plan' of proportional
representation can make every vote effective
—can give every voter a voice in the govern-
ment which he is expected to obey. To
utilise the contingent vote to secure an abso-
lute majority of votes for the successful
candidate is to utilise it to only half its
capacity ; though even that improves upon
a system which continually elects the candi-
dates of a minority to represent a majority.
Like everything that is new and strange, the
application of the contingent vote is very
generally misunderstood. Yet it takes away
no privilege : all that it does is to give an
additional privilege which cannot be too
highly esteemed by those who comprehend
that the true aim of representative institu-
tions is not to secure the rule of a majority,
but to give to majority and minority alike
their due share in the government.
• # *
It is possible that the inveterate sentiment
of locality will, for a long time, prevent the
complete application of proportional voting
to Assembly elections ; but there is no
reason why the system should not be at once
brought into operation to elect a Legislative
Council. In New South Wales that Chamber,
as at present constituted, is an anachronism
and a stumbling-block. Its members are the
nominees of a single man, the Premier of
the province for the time being, since in
practice he has the power of enforcing his
choice upon his colleagues. He has in
general nothing to consider but his own
whims and friendships and the exigencies of
his party, and he packs the Chamber with
the men who have assisted him politically
and financially. Yet this House has the
power to impose an absolute veto upon all
legislation which does not suit the class pre-
judices it so emphatically represents. Short
of absolute despotism, no more undemocratic
ruler could be created.
* • a
To elect a Legislative Council under a
system of proportional representation the
whole province would become a single con-
stituency, local divisions being entirely
abolished. The first result of this would be
to put the purely local candidate, the “road-
and-bridge member,” entirely out of the
contest. A hero only within his own narrow
boundaries, he would have no chance against
men whose names are everywhere household
words. These, not necessarily politicians,
but men who have become known in science,
in art, in literature, would sway only a
minority of votes in any locality. But, with
the whole country a single electorate, the
votes given in their favour would accumu-
late ; and the people would have a chance to
choose a Chamber representing the highest
intelligence of the community. Procedure is
of the simplest. If 300,000 voters had to
elect 100 members, all who received the
quota of 3000 votes at the first count would
be chosen. A second count of contingent
votes would enable other candidates to reach
the quota, and so on till all the seats were
filled. Thus every voter would have a share
in determining the composition of the
Chamber.
* * •
The application of such a plan, by raising
the quality and widening the basis of Parlia-
mentary institutions, would greatly contri-
bute to the value and stability of Govern-
ment. The election of the Executive by
Parliament, with due representation of
parties and a permanent tenure during the
life of Parliament, would prevent, in great
measure, that perpetual struggle for pay and
place which is a most demoralising influence
in our politics. But, as all life tends to
death, so all representative bodies tend to
become unrepresentative, and the people must
hold the referendum as a weapon in reserve.
It is, indeed, a cumbrous w'eapon, not to be
wielded lightly or rashly; but the mere
knowledge of its existence would serve to
make its use for the most part unnecessary.
And both philosophy and experience go to
show that the suggested changes in our
scheme of government, by enabling the
people to participate more fully in its opera-
tions, and by giving greater opportunities
for the advancement of the wisest among us
to the highest positions, will at the same
time promote the general progress of the
community and organise the worth already
existing to co-operate with the greatest effect
on public affairs—thereby realising the poli-
tical ideal which Mill and others have
fondly imagined.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
How the Constitution was Saved.
The fall of the Dibbs Ministry, ostensibly
through the refusal of Gov. Duff to appoint
seven new Legislative councillors nominated by
the Government, has given rise, in the Foreign-
trade press, to a unanimous declaration that
the country has again been saved. The atti-
tude of the shoddy organs is that the Dibbs
Cabinet, being a badly-defeated organisa-
tion, attempted to over-ride all precedents, and


to subvert the Constitution by swamping the
Upper House with its own creatures, and the
Governor, on high constitutional grounds, put
his veto on the outrage. As a matter of fact,
however, there was no attempt to over-ride
precedents, no veto, and no high constitutional
grounds whatsoever. In the first place, the
Dibbs Ministry was not defeated. The direct
Protectionist party is in a minority in the N.S.
Wales Assembly, and so is the direct Foreign-
trade party. Whichever of these parties can
secure the Labour-vote by timely concessions
will come uppermost, and until it was proved
that the Dibbs Government couldn’t, by swal-
lowing its alleged principles or otherwise,
secure the Labour-vote, it was not, in any legal
sense, a defeated Ministry. In fact, its situa-
tion was very much the same as it has been for
three years past ; it has held office for three
years by the grace of the Labour members, just
as the Parkes Ministry did before it; and
there was no evidence before the Governor to
show that it might not do so again. Secondly,
even if he had been defeated, the precedents
were all in Dibbs’ favour. Both in Britain and
Australia, the outgoing Ministry has an evil
habit of creating a few peers in its last hours by
way of rewarding its supporters. The instance
which bears most directly on the case, however,
was that of Premier Atkinson, of Maoriland,
who, when he and his party had been practi-
cally extinguished at the polls, and he was
merely holding office pending the arrival of his
successor, was allowed, the very day before he
finally retired, to shunt about seven fine
old crusted Tories into the Legislative
Council. In this case, Ballance, who
was then virtually Premier, though not
yet sworn in, protested vigorously, but
Governor Onslow held that he must follow the
advice of the existing Ministry; and even the
Foreign-trade press of Sydney expressed a mild
approval—or, at least, uttered no word in dis-
approval—of his action. The precedent was a
hopelessly bad one, and Dibbs, as a presumably
sane individual, must have been aware of the
fact; but the Constitution is made up of prece-
dents, mostly bad ones, and in the recent case
Dibbs’ action was strictly constitutional. As
for the Governor’s position in the matter, it
was feeble and eminently ridiculous. If he
believed the action of the Dibbs Government
t > be unconstitutional, it was his duty to put
down his foot on the entire list of nominations ;
if he believed it to be constitutional, then it
was his business to accept the whole list, other-
wise the pretence that New South Wales is a
self-governing province, so far as its own inter-
nal affairs are concerned, is a mistake from first
to last. Instead, however, he tried to strike a
feeble average between thetwo coursesbyoffering
to accept five nominations out of seven, and by
admitting the right to make five nominations
he entirely gave away the pretence that there
was anything unconstitutional in the matter,
and also gave away his own right to interfere
in any way. Under these hideously confused
circumstances the Government which repre-
sented a minority went out, and another
Government, which also represents a minority,
has come in. But, as for the Constitution, it
hasn’t been saved any more than it was before ;
and the only definite result is that the imperial
figure-head has asserted his right to encroach a
little further upon the self-governing powers of
New South Wales, and has been enthusiastically
supported by the Foreign-trade press in so
doing.
Sydney “ Telegraph's ” Victorian
“Merchant.”
The Sydney Telegraph, inspired by Treasurer
Carter’s alleged Freetrade budget, has been
interviewing “ a prominent Melbourne mer-
chant ” from whom it has extracted much con-
solation. The name and address of the “ mer-
chant ” are not given, nor are there any
particulars available as to the kind of merchan-
dise he deals in ; in fact, we have only the
Telegraph’s assurance that there is any such
person at all. Arid, considering how faithfully
he echoes all the paper’s sentiments, a great
many people will probably suspect that the
“ merchant ” was constructed for the occasion,
and has no existence of his own except when he
is being interviewed by the Telegraph. One
or two of his statements are interesting :
What is the advantage of a duty against the importation
of wheat when there is no importation? Only oats are
imported, as they cannot be produced in Victoria, so the
cabmen and all the peop'e who keep horses have to pay
extra for the imported outs, while the Victorian farmers
eet no benefit from the duty. Victoria is not the climate
tor growing oats.
It will probably stagger the Telegraph and its
“merchant ” to hear that Victoria grows nearly
5,000,000 bushels of oats annually, that the
quantity grown is steadily increasing, and that
the province actually exports this article in
considerable quantities. Therefore, the above
pathos about the oppressed cabmen is pure
bunkum and mendacity. The same merchant
contradicts himself badly in these two state-
ments :
The labouring population of Victoria is more Protec-
tionist than the population of any other part of the world.
• . . There has been a regular revolution (in Victorian
public opinion), and it has all been worked within the
past twelve months.
Then he bobs up serenely again :
Protection has this year driven from 25,000 to 30,000
men out of Melbourne.
Hayter has a vague idea that the population
of Melbourne has remained about stationary
this year, but probably the Telegraph’s
“merchant” knows better. Then he starts
out again:—
The protected industries of Victoria are all moribund.
Considering that the oats above-mentioned are
a protected industry, the “ merchant ” would
have been better advised if he had said nothing
on this subject. Also, he would probably have
done better if he had said nothing on any other
subject. Where on earth does the Telegraph
get that “merchant” who is always so con-
veniently on hand just when he is wanted ?
A Salt Job.
When the Victorian Government committed its
second “ sugar-beet bonus ” bill, a few months
ago, a suggestion was made in The Bulletin
that the salt industry at French Island and
elsewhere had a more legitimate claim to State
encouragement. Since then the French Island
operators have ceased their struggle to make
the salt business lucrative, thus adding another
chapter to the long history of financial failure
at that place. Many thousands of pounds
capital and much labour of hopeful men have
been wasted in the attempts to obtain a success-
ful salt-yield by natural and artificial evapora-
tion from the sea that washes upon the island.
It was regarded as the test spot where a trade
could be established if there were any fair
chance of money in the business. The chance
now appears smaller than ever—an important
fact to note in connection with the unblushing
job about to be perpetrated by the Victorian
Government. There is another salt-gatherer
holding the right to work upon 690 acres of
shore frontage on the outskirts of Geelong pre-
sent, which same tract of coast may be in
the centre of Geelong future. Here, like-
wise, money and labour have been expended
to no purpose, but, in the avowed hope of giving
new life to the failing management, the
Victorian Government has passed a bill to
sanction the sale of the 690 acres outright at
£1 per acre. Many members, of course, spoke
vigorously against the proposed sin. The
Legislative Assembly hasn’t been formed into
one great conspiracy for enriching the inevit-
able syndicate that will presently grab this
valuable freehold property. For instance,
Vale, M.L.A., said, “ The only conclusion he
could arrive at was that there was a job in-
tended to enable certain people to obtain pos-
session of the land, and the possibilities of an
enormous fortune in the future.” But the bill
passed its second reading all right. The
Government was asked to explain why a long
lease of the land at nothing a year would not
meet all the honest requirements of the salt
producers. On the security of a 90, or even 50
years’ lease, they could obtain all the extra
capital they could ever hope to tempt into this
unpromising enterprise, therefore the sale of the
land would plainly be tantamount to an admis-
sion that the deed was done, not for the benefit of
a salt trade, but for the benefit of parties
having ulterior designs in getting possession of
the sea frontage. The Ministry offered
no defence of their determination to alienate
the shore, although one of their supporters, J.
Harris, M.L.A., foolishly laid the matter bare
whilst trying to cover it up. Said Harris :
“ There were two gentlemen connected with
the affair, and they wanted to raise £2OOO more
capital, and they were told that if they got the
freehold they would get £lO,OOO more put into
the concern.” In Heaven’s name, why ? How
could the salt prospects gain by a freeholding,
instead of a leaseholding, of the land ? What
could be the object of sinking £690 of this new
capital in the purchase of a place which they,
as salt-producers, might occupy until the middle
of next century ? Possessing the fee simple of
their salt-works site, the salt-workers could
turn it to profitable account. The business
could be quietly abandoned as a final failure
and then—well, the land contains “ the possi-
bilities of an enormous fortune.” An amend-
ment in favour of leasing was defeated with
great slaughter. Then came a simple proposal
that the deed of sale should be saddled with “ a
provision that the land should only he used for
the purpose of making salt.” The Govern-
ment had either to vote for this, or to devour
their own hollow pretence of wishing to aid a
salt works. They ate their ghastly piece of
humbug like shameless gluttons. Patterson,
O’Loghlen, MTntyre, and the rest of them
voted for not forcing this condition upon the
purchasers. They now stand exposed as would-
be vendors of their country’s sea-shore to a syn-
dicate, at £1 per acre. The bill is not yet
through the House—indeed, the pertinent
amendment referred to was actually carried
against Patterson and Co. by the casting-vote
of Tom Bent. But the Government will do its
worst to sell those 690 acres without the re-
stricting condition, and if the age of miracles
were not past we might have seen Patterson
turned into a pillar of salt by an indignant
Almighty.

Debt.
Debt is the true emblem of immortality. It
goes on for ever and ever, and the ten per shent.
multiplies at simple and compound interest till
it is as incalculable as the sands on the sea-
shore. It is endless, boundless, sublime, in-
finite, and altogether unfathomable, and whither
it is leading the country no one even pretends
to know. But it is generally agreed that some-
thing must be done to alleviate its ravages, and
the Credit Foncier scheme, now being planned
out in Maoriland, is intended to lighten, in
some degree at least, the awful burden of local
liabilities. The following figures will show that
there is abundant room for such an undertaking.
Maoriland, it is estimated, is privately mort-
gaged to the extent of something over
£30,000,000. This figure relates to freehold and
leasehold mortgages only. The average interest
is estimated at 8 per cent., and at this rate
the province pays £2,400,000 per annum, and
keeps on paying it for ever, and yet doesn’t
shift the original liability so much as an inch.
In 25 years, at this rate, it pays away
£60,000,000 as interest upon £30,000,000, and
the debt still remains as ponderous, and as
irremoveable, and as capable of earning another
£60,000,000 of interest, as it was at the begin-
ing. Under the Credit Foncier scheme the
annual charge will be reduced to 5 per cent.,
and by a payment of £7 2s. per cent, per
annum, principal and interest will both be
extinguished in 25 years. In other words, the
province will pay a little over £53,000,000 in a
quarter of a century to get rid of both
principal and interest, whereas it now pays
£60,000,000 in interest only, and the original
millstone of £30,000,000 remains hanging round
its neck as heretofore. The borrowers will thus
save £37,000,000 in 25 years ; and as rather
more than half the debt consists of English
capital, the interest on which is absolutely lost
to the country, the saving to the community at
large will be about £500,000 a year for a quarter
of a century, and about £1,300,000 per annum
through all eternity afterwards. The interest
paid to the Maoriland capitalist is not an abso-
lute loss to the community at large, but the in-
terest paid to the English capitalist never returns,
except to be lent out again and to earn more
interest, and, so far as Maoriland is concerned,
it is as utterly and hopelessly gone as if it had
been shovelled into the Pacific. Any method
which will reduce the amount of this awful
tribute in the immediate present, and gradually
extinguish it in the future, is an unmitigated
blessing to a debt-ridden community, and the
dead statesman who bequeathed this great idea
to his country deserves to have a statue as high
as the pyramids erected over his bones, to keep
his name alive through all the coming genera-
tions.
Of an Injudicious Judge.
All Caul is divided into three parts ; but New
South Wales District Court Judge Gibson is
only divided into two. The Gibson section
may be about 40 years old; the Judge section
may be about 4. When the two sections work
harmoniously, the result is right enough ; but
when the coltish 4-year-old gets loose and
frolics around indiscriminately the result is
awful. Unfortunately, the mature Gibson was
off duty the other day at Broken Hill, when
there was a quarrel and a fight among a crowd
of miners off shift, and one man was brought up
charged with assault. Then, according to the
Barrier Miner, the immature Judge ruffled
his wig and spake : —•
These men behaved like a lot of brutes, yet they have
equal responsibilities with you and me, who are respect-
able members of society— their vote is as good as yours or
mine in the election of Parliamentary representatives.
These are the men who congregate together by fifties and
behave in this disgraceful manner—these are the men the
country has thought fit to give the enormous power of
the vote.
Just here, the presence of an infant Judge cry-
ing in the Court-room becomes painfully
evident. In the absence of nurse Gibson, he
hasdragged two non sequitur chairs to the Parli-
amentary table, and is proceeding to pull his
country’s lamp over. Some men “behave like
a lot of brutessome men “ congregate
together by fifties;” therefore Judge implies
censure upon the country which has given them
“the enormous power of the vote.” When
Judge grows older, The Bulletin trusts Gib-
son will teach him a little logic and something
of economics. Logic will show him that
there is no more connection between “con-
gregation by fifties ” and capacity to vote than
there is between wigs and wisdom. Mere
“congregation by fifties” is perfectly legal;
and, in the case of the Barrier miners, who are
herded together under the modern wage-system
to earn dividends for lawyers and others, it is
perfectly natural. Economics will show Judge
that the system of representative government
under which he is living is a system of govern-
ment by averages. When the community is
averaged it is found that each class in the com-
munity seeks its own interests. When the rich
are in power they make laws to suit the rich ;
when the middle-class are in power they make
laws to suit the middle-class ; if the poor
could possibly achieve power they would make
laws to suit the poor. Government by lawyers
would mean government for lawyers; and, in
order to secure government for the people, we
have government by the people. Only by the
consensus of the whole can the will of the
majority be equitably ascertained. And, on a
philosophic survey, it does not appear that any
class in the community has a moral superiority.
Brutality at the bottom of the social ladder is
more than balanced by vice at the top. Says
Rousseau :
Having found so many good people in my youth, why
do I find so few in my age? Is their race extinct? No ;
but I do got seek them in the same situation. I did
formerly among the “ lower classes,” where violent
passions predominate only at intervals, and where Nature
speaks her genuine sentiments. In more elevated stations
they are entirely smothered, and, under the mask of
sentiment, only interest or vanity is heard.
The quarrelling and fighting which Judge
Gibson condemns are brutal, but natural,
habits ; and it is an open question whether
their destruction is worth the loss of vitality
incurred in adapting the natural man to the
prim pattern of civilisation. Anyhow, nothing
can be clearer than the fact that we owe
the blessing of Judge to the ancestral
Gibson’s exceptional talent for fighting and
quarrelling. If some prehistoric Gibson, in-
stead of braining the other fellow with a big
bone, had let the other fellow brain him, there
would be no necessity for The Bulletin to put
the question whether, after all, the ethical
standard of “brutes” who “congregate in
fifties” and fight is not quite as high as that of
judges who deliberately condone theft when a
lawyer is the thief, or of judges who take
advantage of the political power with which
they have been trusted by the people in order to
plunder the people’s purse ? Here is a problem
in ethics for Judge to puzzle out when he has
learnt logic and economics. By that time,
doubtless, Gibson will have taught him dis-
cretion also, and he will see that it is not the
duty of a Judge to condemn manhood suffrage
in deciding an assault case ; that his office is to
administer the law and not to inveigh against
the Government ; that he has no right, in
punishing one man for a proven offence, to
insult others for unproven offences ; and that
the Judge who does these things nob only mires
his own ermine but also degrades the dignity
and lessens the esteem in which it is necessary
for the public welfare that Justice should be
held.
“ Drift, Dribble, and Bolster the
Banks!”
When the Griflilwraith took office in 1890, the
accumulated Queensland deficit was £969,000.
The Morehead Ministry proposed to balance
revenue and expenditure by a property tax,
which should take something of the burden off
backs and bellies and place it on wealth. The
new Treasurer, Mr. Thomas M'llwraith,
declared that no taxation was necessary, and
his cohort of journalistic sycophants proved
conclusively that with sucli a heaven-born
financier at the helm, credit would be restored
in a twinkling. The consequence was that the
loss on next year’s operations (1890-91) was
£334,431, and the unfortunate province has
been going down-hill ever since. Even when
Boodlewraith ejected an upright Judge and
assimilated the leader of the Opposition, things
failed to mend. The loss for 1891-2 was
£151,564: for 1892-3, £260,741 ; for 1893-4 it
was apparently .£58,467, though, as Mr. Hugh
Nelson’s two last half-yearly balance-sheets do
not agree, this amount must be left doubtful.
The accumulated deficit at June 30, 1894, was
either .£2,090,648 or .£2,140,648 (there is some
hanky-panky with a sum of .£50,000 advanced
to revenue by Treasury Notes, which pops up
in the December balance-sheet and disappears
in that of June). According to the Treasurer’s
statement, the accumulated deficit at June 30
was £295,608 ; though how he makes this out
puzzles earth-born financiers, since, on his own
showing at December 31, 1893, he had a credit
balance of £10,737, and he has lost £165,678 in
the interim, so that his debit balance should
be now only £154,941, Anyhow, this is clear ;
an accumulated deficit of over two millions has
been reduced to a nominal deficit of £300,000, by
(1) transferring £1,450,621 from loan account
to revenue account, and (2) by assisting the
revenue with £274,064, the proceeds of forced
sales of valuable land in large areas at 10s. an
acre. Authority to sell this land at such a low
price was given by a special Act passed in 1891,
which commenced “ Whereas certain Treasury
Bills have heretofore been authorised to be
issued, amounting in all to the sum of
£1,420,945, and it is desirable to make special
provision for retiring such Treasury Bills, be it
enacted,” &c. The plain purpose of this was
that the proceeds of the land sold should be
devoted to meeting Treasury Bills. The
Treasurer, however, took advantage of a per-
missive clause, and collared £274,000 to
straighten up revenue shortages ; and this year,
when it is illegal so to collar any more, he con-
gratulates the province in his Budget speech
that he has been able to apply no less
than £13,355 of further proceeds to the
reduction of the accumulated deficit. As
to the borrowing of the £1,450,621 slumped
into the revenue to make ends meet, the
Queensland Treasurer is simply adopting the
time-honoured expedient of bankrupt mer- f
chants who fly kites to keep the business afloat.
He has increased the public debt by nearly 1J
million and the annual interest on the public
debt by £58,000, and now comes forward to
advocate flotation of a fresh loan on the ground
that he can show a square balance ! And all
this time Queenslanders have been living hard
and working hard to increase wealth and popu-
lation. Since 1890 the population has increased
from 385,000 to 432,000. Since 1890 the total
imports have decreased from £5,066,000 to
£4,347,000. Since 1890 the total exports have in-
creased from £8,554,000 to £9,614,000. Figures
could not speak more plainly of hard times
and hard struggle. Excluding live stock, the
people who imported goods to thevalueof £l2los.
per head in 1890 could only afford to import the
worth of £9 15s. per head in 1893; the people
who exported goods worth £l7 3s. per head in
1890, exported the worth of £2O 12s. per head
in 1893. Yet with this surplus of income over
outgo, Queensland goes steadily to leeward
under the grand Government policy of Drift,
Dribble and Bolster the Banks ! The province
is eaten up with interest, throttled in the clutch
of the usurer. According to Statistician
Coghlan’s recent tables, in the two years
1891 and 1892, no less than £8,014,000 was
sent out of Queensland in excess of capital
introduced. That’s where the leak is. And to
stop it, Treasurer Nelson proposes more loans,
more interest and more leak! These considera-
tions enable us to understand how it is that in
Queensland, with a population of 400,000, a
debt of 32 millions, and an annual charge on
less than 3i million revenue of more than 1J
million interest, wages are so high, work is so
plentiful, and the countiy is so confoundedly
prosperous.
Wanted—Guarantors for Lawyers.
The latest case of a Melbourne “ solicitor in
trouble ” seems rather more humorous than the
average. Robert Knox Orme was summoned
to appear before Judge A’Beckett, at the
instance of the Law Institute, to show cause
why he should not be reprimanded for “mis-
conduct.” That is to say, the gentleman was
now coming up for his Honor’s reserved judg-
ment, the acts having been fully explained on a
previous occasion. They were hideously simple
facts for a Judge to go home and wrestle with,
to be sure, but it is not etiquette on a Judge’s
part to perceive anything cronk in a solicitor
until he has pondered the matter a long time.
The “misconduct” of a legal brother doesn’t
dawn upon a Judge in a hurry, even if it dawns
upon him at all, and then, as a rule, it doesn’t
dawn much. R. K. Orme, solicitor, pocketed
the sum of £3l 10s., “ paid to him for a specific
purpose.” With this sum he was instructed
to wipe off the balance of liability upon a time-
payment piano, Orme himself having advised
the payment of said balance, for reasons apper-
taining to a certain auction-sale. As soon as
he got the £3l 10s. for the purpose that he had
professionally recommended, R. K. Orme de-
voted it to another specific purpose—viz.,
his own bank - account. Then he drew
upon the £3l 10s., and did eat and drink
and enjoy himself until there was only
£l5 of it left to tell the awful tale. At this
time the bank suspended payment, but—mark
the judge’s words ! —“ he obtained the balance
on the representation, unfortunately true, that
it was trust money.” Whereupon R. K. Orme,
solicitor, spent the aforesaid balance. Mean-
time, the unfortunate piano was sold to an
unfortunate bookmaker, as an article which
had been relieved of all liability. The book-
maker found that the aforesaid debt of £3l
10s. was still outstanding, and had to pay it
off himself. When he applied to the auctioneer
for restitution, the auctioneer (insolvent) could
only refer him to the crooked solicitor: So
the bookmaker sued R. K. Orme in the County
Court, without any result save costs. Then he
proceeded against R. K. Orme on a fraud
summons, and still there was no result other
than more costs. R. K. Orme was not ordered
to pay up in instalments or go to gaol because
he pleaded absolute poverty. Clients seemingly
had ceased to feed his banking account, and he
left the County Court with his thumb to his
nose, metaphorically speaking. Then the Law
Institute actually became very cross indeed
with brother R. K. Orme— perhaps the book-
maker insisted upon “taking it out” of the
solicitor somehow —-and the appeal to Justice

A'Bbckett followed. The culprit’s comic
“ defence ” is shown in the following extract
from the A’Beokett judgment: —
Ormb had smrsrested that had the bank not suspended
payment ha have been able to get an overdraft
Sufficient to pay off this amount, but that did not affect
the deliberate appropriation of money to his own purpose
which was given to him for another purpose.
Stern justice had to be meted out to Orme for
his “misconduct.” “His Honor made an order
that Orme should pay the costs (Hee-haw !) of
this application ; that he be suspended from
practice for 12 months, and for an additional
period of two years unless ” —he paid these
costs as well as the -£3l 10s. which he had em-
bezzled. The learned Judge said “ misappro-
priated” for embezzled, and he did not say any-
thing about the costs incurred by the book-
maker in his fruitless proceedings to recover
the £3l 10s. The honour of the legal profes-
sion is easily upheld. Orme will be again
reckoned a worthy member of the cult at the
end of 12 months, if he can raise a few pounds
by that time. A business agent who misap-
propriated money entrusted to him for a specific
purpose, and who went to gaol as the result of
so doing, would be, by the Court’s admission,
quite good enough to enter the honourable pro-
fession of the law after doing his sentence.
Seeing that a character for honesty is not
necessary to members of this closely-
protected industry, it may occur to some
people that lawyers ought at least to
be “guaranteed” to the extent of a few
hundreds of pounds, even as a brewery or
drapery collector of accounts is guaranteed.
There should surely be a little protection for
clients. At present nobody can entrust a £5
note to a lawyer without entertaining a
suspicion that it might as well be rolled up into
a pellet and dropped down the sink-hole. The
Government requires a money guarantee against
the possibility of dishonesty in a civil servant,
and a civil servant is educated to a higher code
of honour than a lawyer is.
Probably the most frantic epistle which even
Parkes ever dug out of his inner consciousness
is the one which he heaved at the astonished
head of M'Millan in last Friday’s papers.
That peaceful little draper invited Parkes,
along with the rest of the Foreign-trade party,
to attend a banquet in honour of Premier Reid,
and this last straw broke the back of Parkes’s
patience. In the awful blast of his reply he
overwhelmed Reid with several tons of mud,
and having sized him up generally as an ass
and an impostor, he sent back the invitation,
intimating that a banquet to such a degraded
person was the fittest place for M‘Millan.
Which was evidently meant to intimate that
M‘Millan was only fitted to carry entrails to
a bear, and that Reid was eminently adapted
to have entrails carried to him by M‘Millan.
Parkes in his dotage is a truly lamentable
spectacle. When he threw up the leadership
of the Foreign-trade party three years ago, he
did it in the evident expectation that the party
would crawl before him on its stomach and
implore him to come back. When it did
so, and he flatly refused to come back, he
evidently expected that it would keep on crawl-
ing, and he enjoyed seeing it in a horizontal
attitude. Later on, when Reid, in a loose and
effusive moment at a banquet, offered to resign
the leadership to Parkes at any moment, the
old man simply grinned amid the fastnesses of
his hair and made no sign ; he was pleased to
see Reid flatten himself in the dust, and meant
to let him keep on doing it till he told the
wallowing object to get up. But at last the
Foreign-trade party found that it could get on
just as well without Parkes, and ceased wallow-
ing in the dirt; and since then the old man’s
fury has grown more and more awful, until at
last he can keep up the outward semblance of
decency no longer, and feels that he must jump
on little Cobden’s feeding-bottle to relieve his
feelings, or else burst. The next probable
move will be that Parkes will hold out the
wrinkled hand of friendship to Dibbs, and talk
in a husky undertone about a coalition, with
himself as Premier. Then Dibbs, who is also
a polite letter-writer of note, will probably offer
him the Postmaster-Generalship in their pros-
pective Cabinet, and Parkes will retire to his
home and concoct such an epistle as was never
seen before outside Chinatown. There are
immense possibilities in the situation, for the
hairy bard is evidently in such a state of mind
that he must do something.

Dear Bulletin, —The big church advt. which
Card, Moran dexterously contrived out of
Carnot’s death included an address in which
he calmly tied science, civilisation, morality
and a few other things to the coat-tails of St.
Peter. After drawing a fine contrast between
the civilised Christians and the barbarous
Saracens, he proceeded to glorify “ the Divine
mission ” of Joan of Arc, the saviour of
“Catholic France.” Now, if there was any
mission about the matter, that of the English
was plainly as divine as that of Joan, since the
English were related to Joan as cause
to effect. Without one there could have
been no t’other. But there is no more
warrant for seeking a theological ex-
planation of the trouble at Orleans than
there is for bringing a machine-god to account
for the defeat of Dibbs. Joan was simply a
hysterical girl, like the Enmore stone-thrower,
or the Salem witch-finders. She “ heard
voices ” —so did Deeming and lots of other
people in whose proceedings the Divine finger
has, so far, not been recognised. She lived in
an age of intellectual savagery, when grown
men believed in the Kingdom of Spooks as
devoutly as little children to-day believe in the
Black Man. Joan managed to convince people
that her particular Spook could knock out the
rival English Spook ; her hysteria generated
enthusiasm, enthusiasm bred confidence, and
voild tout. The French leaders hadn’t much faith
in her “mission,” and disregarded her orders in
battle ; but they were glad to make use of her
Spook. English carelessness, born of success,
helped the first defeat, and the knowledge that
Joan was a witch did the rest. If they had
only been able to hunt up a reliable witch to
barrack for their side with a Spook, France
might be an English province still. Of course,
this may be “the finger of God” again, for
England was destined to be Protestant, and
Protestantism never had much luck with its
Spooks, comparatively. When by-and-by the
English caught Joan, they were disgusted to
find what a take-in of a witch she was. But
the mischief was done then; so they consoled
themselves by doing for Joan. Her Spook ran
away and left her; so like a Spook.—Yours,

Unwholesome Smellbourne is, for the moment*
the scene of a disheartening cant struggle
between two itinerant preachers. M’Neill,
the ’Murkanised Scotchman, has been over-
taken by Canon Carter, an exceedingly
Hinglish divine, who states, “ without con-
scious reference ” to anybody else, that the
other evangelist is calculated to “produce
disastrous consequences.” The chances are that
neither noisy M‘Neill nor the higher-toned
canon will personally produce any kind of
consequences worth mentioning, but the fact
that their religions are violently opposed should
enable a consequence to evolve itself from the
conflict. One is a rabid spiritual vaccinationist,
declaring that the fat patient who “takes
well ” obtains eternal happiness. The other
deprecates the vaccination of “ unawakened
sinners,” on the ground that it merely
brings them out in nasty sores without pro-
tecting them from damnation. Of these two
doctrines an anti-vaccinationist naturally pre-
fers the latter, although he doesn’t prefer it
with much enthusiasm. The “ Christianity ”
that admits of such a startling difference of
opinion in its professors is a doubtful religion
for any pupil to swear by. There must be
something radically wrong with a faith which is
declared to be the right faith under certain
conditions only, and something very absurd
about a slow repentance which, according to
the other man, is not genuine unless you rush it
through your immortal soul. If one sort of
Terewth be unsound, its blood relation is pro-
bably tainted with the same disease. By these
discordant yells of “Believe this!” and
“ Believe that !” the true unbeliever is re-
minded of Disraeli’s answer to the curious
enquirer: “Every wise man has a religion,
but no wise man says what it is.”
Morality is of two kinds—offensive and defen-
sive. Defensive morality sits at home minding
its business, making a happy fireside clime for
children and wife, and doing its best to pluck
the beam out of its own eye. Offensive morality
flaunts a Pharisaical phylactery in higiiways
and byways, pokes its nose into everybody’s
tater-patch, and not content with going to its
own peculiar heaven in its own peculiar way,
insists that everybody else shall copy its
snuffle. Mow, when Queensland Minister Tozer
was younger, he once got a very great fright ;
and ever since his public morality has been
most offensive. So when a football match was
played at Charters Towers on a recent Sunday,
he wired up :
This introduction of Continental method of public
sports on Sunday is notin accord with national senti-
ment, and has not the approval of the Government. It
must be stopped.
But public opinion on the Towers is divided
between two classes known as Tartars and
Littlejohnnycorkers; and, unfortunately, Tozer
caught a Tartar in John Marsland, who wired
back :
With exception of very few religious maniacs here,
public feeling entirely in favour of Sunday playing, which I
respectfully point out is strictly in accordance with law,
the concourse of persons being within their own parish.
As Marsland is a lawyer who knows his busi-
ness, Tozer reluctantly caved, stating :
Will not interfere further, but shall certainly recom-
mend such action as will prevent this and other public
grounds from being used in future for purposes so detri-
mental to the best interests of the colony.
Here, you see, Tozer saves Queensland the
trouble of thinking by deciding at once what is
and is not good for it. The ground in question
is expressly set apart as a reserve for athletic
purposes ; and as long as their sport is con-
ducted without disorder, the people of Charters
Towers have every right to choose for them-
selves whether they will enjoy recreation in the
open air or go to see an owl sitting on a dead
branch of the tree of knowledge and hooting
the same old hoot about the same old hell. The
Towers is so near hell all the week that no
sensible person will blame residents for trying
to dodge it on Sundays. But Tozer isn’t
sensible ; he’s swollen. Has anybody a pin ?
It is interesting just now to hunt up the “great”
Sj’dney dailies for July, 1891, and compare their
utterances then with those of the past week or
two. At that time a general election had left
th® Parkes Foreign-trade Government in a bad
minority as compared to the Protectionist
Opposition, but it proposed to meet Parliament
in the hope that, by introducing or promising
certain democratic measures, it would gain
enough Labour votes to keep it in office. Now
the general election has left the Dibbs Pro-
tectionist Government in a bad minority as
compared with the Foreign-trade Opposition,
but it proposed to skirmish in exactly the
same way for the Labour vote to beep
it in office. But, though the circum-
stances were the same, the Telegraph
and the Herald have been whooping in an
altogether different key. Then they implored
Parkes to hold on, and pointed out that he
couldn’t be officially acquainted with the state
of parties until Parliament met and he was
confronted by a direct vote of censure or
otherwise, and that it would be “unconstitu-
tional ” if he resigned until he was actually
thrown out. And now that Dibbs has been
proposing to take the same rather undignified
course, the same Herald and Telegraph
denounced him as an unprincipled grabber after
office and a shameless individual who wouldn’t
let go his billet until dragged off it by main
force. The Star has lately been digging up
these articles in which the Calico Jimmy brigade
was urged to hold on to the bitter end, and
publishing them side by side with the political
shrieks of the last week or two. The compari-
son is still another evidence that the late
lamented Ananias was an editor.
Not a ray of hope illuminates the Yic. political
outlook. The exchange of Patterson for
Shiels would merely place a vacillating, thin-
skinned, undiscerning word-spinner in the seat
of a time-serving, coarse-hided, impudent
blufler. Nor would the colleagues of the
younger man carry more respect than the
associates of the arrogant “old hand.” They
all belong to the House that has con-
nived at the escape of criminals and
laughed at the prostitution of justice, on the
pretence that all was for the best. Among the
few members who have boldly advocated decent
dealing with scandals, there is not one likely
to be chosen for a place in any new Ministry
unless the country makes a sweeping change in
the Assembly at next election. Which the
country is tolerably certain not to do. It has
§rown accustomed to the supreme stink of its
enate, and like the nasty fellow in the nasty
story it “ likes t’ould smell best.”
Here we Are—at Last!
New South Wales now knows the worst. It
has before it the first creation of the Freetrade
clown, and possibly the last caper of the Free-
trade pantaloon. The first Ministry of George
Houston Reid emerged into the light of
existence from the dim drawing-room of Burde-
kin, the millionaire. The first Ministry which
starts to tax out of existence the unearned incre-
ment of the land-monopolist is shown to the
door of its political existence by the flunkey of
a man who, above all, battens on land monopoly.
Hitherto G. H, Reid has been looked upon as a
dilletante politician, fond of a joke, fond of
lounging round in evening dress, and regarding
the serious work of politics through a distant
and rose-coloured eyeglass. His legislative
career had nothing to show for it but an Act for
Regulating the Width of Streets and Lanes,
which was probably a selfish measure inspired
by his own growing girth. He occasionally
wandered into Parliament from a Govern-
ment Hduse dinner and fired off a speech
which showed cleverness. He is known
as a good after-dinner man, but the
country has never yet had a chance
of seeing what he can do before dinner. Offered
the vacant leadership of the Freetrade-party,
he accepted, gave up evening-dress in the
House, allowed the eye-glass to fall into desue-
tude, and angled for the Labour-vote by simu-
lating a violent attack of Democracy. This
attack was accelerated by the total abolition of
700 solid plural club voters in East Sydney.
His rival, Parkes, treated Reid’s leadership as
a joke, and regarded his own position as that of
a nobleman who sends his butler before him to
secure sumptuous apartments at a hotel, which
butler, after going to all the trouble, is relegated
to the kitchen while his master takes possession
of the apartments. The people of New South
Wales have taken the joke seriously, and the
result is—the Reid Ministry.
* • »
It is difficult to say which is the most sur-
prising—the names of the Ministers or the
offices to which they have been appointed.
First comes Mr. Reid himself—Premier and
Colonial Treasurer, pledged to the hilt to take off
the specific and ad valorem duties and stick on a
land-tax to replace them as a source of revenue.
That’s a big contract, and it will be in-
teresting to watch him doing it. Brunker,
the cherubic, inoffensive ex-Minister of Lands,
inherits the mantle of Colonial Secretary
lately worn by the headstrong Dibbs. A mild,
benevolent, honest gentleman is Brunker. As a
stock-salesman, it must be a pleasure to any
sheep to be sold by him. As a Minister of
Lands, a success according to his old-fashioned
lights. In the office of Colonial Secretary, a
square man in a round hole. Young, Minister
for Works, is a nervous, hesitating man, who
has failed in two fields so widely divergent as a
butter-and-cheese agency and the Speakership
of the Legislative Assembly. He is one of
Parkes’ discoveries, and, unlike many of the
old man’s other finds, has the virtue of honesty,
tempered by stupidity. He owes his Minis-
terial life-breath to Parkes. Oarruthers—
Lands ! Probably the only experience the
dapper little lawyer has had with lands was in
connection with a building society. Another of
Parkes’ discoveries, he also cheerfully deserts
his creator for his rival. Gould —Justice ; a
Tory lawyer, a pillar of the Established Church,
and a political nonentity. It is impossible to
remember anything about him, as he has done
nothing memorable. He hasn’t even the nega-
tive virtue of having left anything important
undone, which he ought to have done. Pass on
to Simpson, Attorney-General, and here we
have a monumental example of extreme correct-
ness of deportment, combined with suavity of
temper, rectitude, and political and forensic
level-headedness. Here is a man who will
always do the right thing in the right way, and
whatever he does will appear right—even
though it were to eat peas with a knife. How-
ever, Mr. Simpson did yeoman service for
Democracy when the Upper House attempted
to steal the Royal metals in the Mining on
Private Lands Bill. He, with ex-Judge Man-
ning and others, frustrated the attempt of the
Upper House to perpetrate that robbery of the
people of New South Wales, and The Bulletin
is pleased to acknowledge the service. The
Vice-President of the Executive Council, Mr.
W. H. Suttor, arouses no enthusiasm, excites
no opposition. He is in the Ministry because
it seems impossible to govern New South Wales
without a Suttor and a deficit. Like the poor,
the Suttors are always with us. In the days
of. King James and Pious William, it was
reckoned diplomatic to have one son a Protes-
tant and another a Roman Catholic, so that,
whichever side turned up trumps, the property
confiscation made no difference. So we have
the spectacle of two Suttors, one a Free-
trader and the other a Protectionist, and
whichever policy comes uppermost, it only re-
sults in a change of initials to the reign-
ing Suttor. Sydney Smith, Mines and
Agriculture, resumes his old position. He
is a “ calm, inscrutable wirepuller ” in his
way, and has cleverly whipped up his men
more than once. He was a railway servant, and,
consequently, knows a lot about mines, and is
very strong on agriculture. However, he stuck
to his guns on the Eight Hour Clause of the
Coal Mines Regulation Bill, when Parkes and
M'Millan threw him over and office up at one
and the same time. Jacob Garrard, Public
Instruction, an erstwhile Protectionist, and
for awhile Minister for Public Works, a
strenuous, strident man, an O’Sullivan in
Scotch, who would have been a good Works
Minister, but in the Education Depart-
ment is as much at home as a French-
man in kilts. As an old trades-unionist
he may be expected to undo much mischief
done by his predecessors in the Technical
College line. “ Somebody must be last ”
Postmaster-General Cook, ‘late Labour-leader.
What shall be said of this appointment? The
P.M.G.-ship appears to be a sort of postscript
in ministry-making. It would seem as if,
in the agonies of Cabinet-making, someone had
remarked to Premier Reid, “Don’t spoil the
ship for a pennyworth of tar,” and Cook had
been called in as the pennyworth of tar. If
his call is to be regarded as a sop to the Labour
Cerberus, it will only make that animal ramp
round with jealousy and rage. Or can it be
that Single-taxer Cook is to be regarded as a
hostage to land-taxation ?

Anyhow, there’s the Ministry to which the
Government of New South Wales is en-
trusted for an indefinite period, and with
Parkes looming dangerously in the near future,
Dibbs, raw and hungry for the gore of the
Governor and the Governor’s new choice, and
the pledged labour league chain-gang anxious
to dbtablish its bona-Jides by voting agin some
Government, Mr. George Houston Reid
should not find it long before he again sits
opposite the ladies’ gallery on the Speaker’s left
hand, glaring at a Coalition Government
through a persuasive eyeglass, a sadder and a
thinner man.

political supplanter!
Ex-Chief Justice Alfred Stephen (N.S.W.)
will enter upon his 93rd year on 20th inst. He is
possibly the oldest living colonist.
A connecting-link with the birth of Mel-
bourne passed over last week at Hurstville, near
Sydney, in the person of John R. N. Batman,
aged 70 years, nephew of John Batman, who
shares with John Pascoe Fawkner the at present
doubtful honour of having founded the capital of
Victoria.
The Bulletin doesn’t marvel at the
agonised Parkes snapping wildly at M’Millan,
for M'Millan it was who, with Bruce Smith, first
put the political bit into Parkes’ mouth. The last
Parkes Ministry could not have existed a month
but for Bruce Smith rind M'Millan aforesaid,
who, remembering the sorts of people with whom
Parkes had allied himself a year or two before
(Garrett, Burns, Ginger Clarke, Abigail), refused
to take office unless practically allowed to nomi-
nate their own colleagues.
When Babbling Reid was first in office as
Vic. Minister of Defence, he had occasion to be
present at a review, where, it happened, a body of
N.S.W. lancers were also present, they being, as
visitors, placed in the position of honour. Reid
drove on to the ground, and making straight for
the most prominent detachment, which happened
to be the Northerners, he rose in his carriage and
told them they were a magnificent body of men,
and as his men he was proud of them, and they
were a credit to Victoria and a far superior body
of men to any that the other provinces could turn
out. Then he drove on as the Sydneyites smiled
audibly to themselves and the local swashbucklers
squirted tobacco-juice about to express their con-
tempt for their new boss.
