Nellie Stewart’s reappearance at Sydney
Lyceum, on Saturday night, was a great occasion,
second only in popularity to the day of the general
resurrection. There was not a vacant seat in the
whole big house, or a vacant step in one of the
gangways, or a vacant spot of standing room any-
where whence one could catch the faintest glimmer
of Nellie’s back hair, or the top of her hat, or any
other part of her adorable person. The subject of
all this excitement looks as young and comely as
she did when she first became a celebrity, and she
has added certain quaint and graceful touches to
the rather commonplace character of Yvonne
which almost disguise its dismal ordinariness.
Her voice, too, was in good condition on Saturday
night, and even after the least attractive of her
songs the audience howled out a voluble encore.
The great heart of Sydney is evidently as true to
Nellie as it ever was. Even the fact that “Paul
Jones ” is only an average burlesque at the best,
and that the average burlesque is a very
long-drawn-out piece of fooling, passed alto-
gether unnoticed in the general enthusiasm.
As for Wallace Brownlow, in the character
of the clean-shaven pirate, Jones, Sydney
will postpone serious consideration of him
until he appears in some part which gives him
more room to let himself out. Miss Vi. Varley’s
Ghopinette is an altogether satisfactory little girl,
who only gets one chance in the whole show and
makes the most of it. This little lady’s brief skirt
pervades the opera a good deal, but the audience
would like to hear her voice oftener. Lauri is a
good substitute for Snazelle as Bouillabaisse, but,
as for the Insect, he is overshadowed by memories
of George Leitch ; there never has been another
Insect to compare with Leitch, and in all proba-
bility there never will be.
* * «
John Gourlay, as the Spanish Governor,
is the one unspeakable joke of “ Paul Jones,” and
he comes to the rescue just when the piece
threatens to get bogged in a morass of sentiment.
Gourlay has the last act almost all to himself and
makes it a wild, weird shriek of awful merriment
right to the end. Most of his doings are apropos
of nothing in particular save himself, and he
creates a general and very gratifying impression
that the play has got the bit between its teeth
and bolted. Tapley is tuneful and pleasing as a
red-haired character of no special importance, and
Howard Vernon disports himself in the unwonted
guise of a stout person who hasn’t much to do.
The chorus, the scenery, and all the fixtures,
fittings, utensils and appurtenances are excellent.
Next Saturday “Mdlle. Nitouche,” a musical
comedy of French origin, and the much-advertised
tahleaux-vivants, will fill the bill.
* * * *
Mr. Garner’s comedy co. debuted at the
Criterion with “Dr. Bill,” a comedy which has
long since been done to death in Sydney, and
wants a long rest for purposes of recovery. It has
been played by so many companies, and viewed
from so many moral and immoral standpoints,
that the man doesn’t live who could find anything
newer original to say concerning it. Mr. Garner’s
company tackles the piece with all the energy
which could be expected, which statement doesn’t
commit this paper to any alarming extent. Mr.
H. Diver, as Dr. Bill, is a capable and almost
brilliant comedian, and the rest of the co. supports
him about as well as the circumstances admit.
But, with the exception of Miss Hilda Fraser,
they are obviously haunted with an oppressive
consciousness that the glory of “ Bill ” departed
long, long ago. Therefore, The Bulletin post-
pones any serious comment upon them until they
appear in some piece which is a shade less
venerable and doesn’t weigh so heavily on the
spirits. “Friends ” on Saturday.
* * * *
The “Lion’s Heart,” which began to
transpire on Saturday at Sydney Majesty’s, is one
long shriek of triumph for G. Rignold from first to
last. The massive Henry V. overshadows every-
body else. He is a lion-tamer by profession, and,
though he doesn’t actually tame any lions on the
stage, it is generally understood that he is pre-
pared to seize a crocodile by the throat at the
shortest notice and hurl it right across the
orchestra into the stalls, and that he constantly
grabs elephants by the hair and heaves them over
cathedrals and other obstructions. By dint of
padding and high-heeled boots and a robust up-
standing hat Mr. Rignold looks about seven feet
high, and nearly as much across. As for his
doings, they are stupendous. He wipes up the
stage with Albert Marsh, and when eight British
yokels try to restrain his fury he scatters them all
with one heave. He mops up most of New Cale-
donia with the prostrate form of H. Plimmer, and
he grabs the gorgeous deputy-governor and throws
him a distance of eight feet. When he isn’t
actually visible, it is generally understood that he
is somewhere in the background tearing up oak-
trees with his teeth and belting down rooky moun-
tains with a club. Altogether, he is a tremendous and
awe-inspiring circumstance. The balance of the
drama is composed mainly of a bogus French
c >lonel and his accomplice, who go about devasta-
te g the earth, and committing murder, perjury,
forgery, abduction, robbery with violence and
larceny as a bailee. Scot Inglis, as the colonel,
is a lovely and picturesque mash, and his uniform
reveals the hitherto-unsuspected fact that he is
even ahead of Ryley in the matter of legs; while
Albert Marsh is a plain and convincing villain
with two crooked extremities, and the manners
and customs of a clean-shaved Mephistopheles.
♦ • * *
These two persons contrive between them
to get the ever-weeping Miss Watts-Phillips
sentenced to life-long penal servitude in New
Caledonia, where that lady leads a damp existence
'u cell 24 for six long years, and can be heard
moaning dreadfully through the keyhole. She
cheers up somewhat, later on, but is almost as
depressing in her cheerful moods as she is
while occupying cell 24. Finally, she turns
out to be the lion-tamer’s lost daughter,
who was stolen in infancy by the party with the
crooked legs, and after she has been torn from
her husband, and married to her own father, and
chased by soldiers through a tangled forest, and
nearly abducted by the colonel, G. Rignold comes
in carrying a dying fever-patient, who explains
everything, including the murder and the stolen
necklace, and all is well. Miss Emma Chambers
and Messrs. Fabert and Plimmer —the latter
gentleman being then somewhat lame through
having been assaulted by the aggressive lion-tatner
and swung round by the hair and pounded against
the furniture—have some vague connection with
tins effective denouement. When it is over, Mr,
Rignold retires, and roots up trees and shifts
immense rocks in a chastened spirit for the rest of
nis life.
** * *
Pastor Talmage, the bounding brother
from Brooklyn, has been holding forth throughout
the week with such brilliant results that at last his
show slopped over into Sydney Town Hall for
want of space elsewhere. His most successful
lecture was that on evolution, entitled “ Where did
we come from?” in the course of which he proved, to
his own entire satisfaction, that we came from some
place where a horrible Murkan accent runs wild in
the woods, and where an untamed preacher twists
himself into dreadful shapes by way of emphasis,
tannage doesn t improve noticeably on further
acquaintance. His cheerfulness is suggestive of
the ghoulish vitality of Count Fosco, and his
mode of reasoning is primitive, and consists
in laying down a statement with an aged joke on
0 P then smiling a cavernous smile and
suddenly wriggling his lean frame into six or
eight unearthly contortions which have no visible
bearing on the case. The Bulletin has come to
the conclusion that Talmage, as Trocadero in
Paul Jones,’' would be an unrivalled success,
and that the Rev. John Gourlay, if he lectured
on “ The Bright Side of Things,” would take
remarkably well. At latest, the eminent
“ divine ” was starting to contort his lean shape
in other Australian cities, beginning at Brisbane,
and gradually wriggling allround the continent.
** * *
That primitive youth, Barnwell, is doing
a second week in Melb. Royal gaol, for the benefit
of the toiling masses. Invitations to various
trades were issued by Mr. Coppin, and accepted
in a humble spirit by such as recognise the possi-
bility of going astray, and the advantages of being
hanged in consequence. “ George Barnwell,”
with all its tawdry sentiment and ludicrous
defects, has endured, and been endured, for about
150 years, so there was no reason why the haughty
bank-clerks of Smellbourne should have treated
Mr. Ooppin’s invite with contumely. They would
be none the worse for hearing Barnwell moralise
in cheap verse. Moreover, there are bank-
managers and other birds of prey who might
profit by the famous speech of the auburn
temptress upon vicious cause and effect. The
present writer admits to a sneaking regard for
this simple story. It has taught him to flee
from the tall lady who wants to weep upon
his shoulder, and he has decided not to murder
another uncle, come what may. The last-named
good resolution is the fruit of a gallows tableau
added to “ G. 8.” since the opening night. The
Bulletin’s original suggestion has been carried
into gruesome effect. After the muffin-bell has
summoned George to execution, the back wall of
the dungeon is rent in twain and a vision of the
law’s dread sentence appears. Young Majeroni,
with a rope round his neck, is beheld standing on
a dim platform beside a figure purporting to be
Miss Williamson, and the hangman awaits his cue
from the parson to jerk the lever. It is quite an
awesome spectacle, and nobody laughs. “Kath-
leen Mavourneen,” which followed “ Barnwell” on
Saturday, affords the further terrible picture of
Charles Holloway as an Irish villain. Holloway
is engaged by a superior monster to slay the
heroine with his fatal accent. Asa preliminary
effort he digs a grave for the young person,
but Sweeney arrives in time to wipe out
Holloway and all his guilty accomplices. At this
point the drama looks rather bad for Sweeney,
because his enthusiasm has carried him too far.
Then the heroine awakens from a previous dream
to the cheerful fact that she never married the
monster in big boots at all, and the gaping grave
and the wholesale homicide turn out to bo the
result of eating pickled walnuts in the first act.
These stage-dreams are becoming a disease.

To-morrow (Friday) the imperturbable
Sass makes one last final appearance, at the
Royal, in a mixed programme, devised for the
benefit of some local charity by an author who
has a comedietta to produce. On Saturday, the
only Darrell comes along with “The Crimson
Thread.” The ever-plucky George appears by
“ special arrangement,” because he never appears
any other way, and the existing company will
support him in his latest work, which includes
things never before attempted on any stage.

At the Alexandra they produced “East
Lynne ” on Saturday, according to threat. Also
the drooping hopes of the management were
revived by a rush of audience, and when Lady
Isabel expired she was placed upon the floor in
such a way that one of her beautiful limbs arrested
the wandering eye and held it fast. The limb
was not the least striking feature in Miss Lilia
Wilde’s acceptable performance, although it had
not been rehearsed and was quite unpremeditated
at the time. An unappalling landslip and an
apotheosis of little Willie are hurled into “East
Lynne,” but they will scarcely avert the inevi-
table for long. The end of Dan. Barry’s season at
the Alexandra seems fairly due. Daniel has
accomplished 20 dramas there, more or less, and if
he now retires on his laurels he may repeat his
difficult feat at some future time. The great art
in managing this theatre is to get away at the
right moment. Meanwhile, extensive alterations
of the Haleck interior and its approaches are
being talked about.
** * *
“ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, is not in high favour with
sassiety or the gods, but business on the ground-
floor has not slackened. Opinions as to the edu-
cational bearings of Paula’s domestic career are
so conflicting that no jury of 12 could knock out
an honest verdict if they retired to the refresh-
ment-room until the sun’s fire went out. On one
point, though, most people can agree. Pinero’s
play is a death blow to Ibsenolatry in English-
speaking countries. The narrow Scandinavian
philosopher has tried in vain to do what
this more worldly wise and poetic dramatist has
accomplished at his second attempt. Mrs. Tan-
queray, treated by the Northern egotist, would
have been twice as verbose, five times as un-
pleasant, and not half as convincing. There is no
question as to the genuineness of Pinero’s
object-lady. Also, there is no doubt that
she must have worried Tanqueray under any
circumstances. But, then, wives in general do
that. Tanqueray’s first venture, the marble pillar
of virtue, seems to have been an incentive to
suicide. The average husband saddens the life of
the women he thought he adored, and Mrs. Jones
occasionally puts arsenic in her tea because the
children have an unsatisfactory father. Where is
a suggestion of matrimonial success in this play ?
Mrs. Oortelyon says nothing about her late hus-
band, so we are at liberty to assume that she
worried him into his grave, in which case “ The
Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” is probably a defence of
free-love principles and an artful plea for good
old Brigham Young, deceased. Mrs. Brough’s
performance is always good and sometiipes better.
The other principals are likewise admired in their
respective parts, but admiration for Captain
Ardale is not very pronounced.
* *- *
Two nights last week were honoured by
Mrs. John Madden’s entertainment at the Alex-
andra, in aid of certain charities. The entertain-
ment consisted of tableaux, and ballads, and an
act of “ Les Cloches,” by the Lyric Club. It gave
great satisfaction to the performers, and to some
of their nearest relations. For the stranger who
took the show in an unbiassed spirit it was a bitter
experience of the awful uses to which amateur
incompetence may be put by an amateur stage-
manager. This remark applies chiefly to the
operatic infliction. The tableaux were simply
inartistic, whilst the ballads ranged from pleasing
to dreadful—p. to and. A Miss Madden got down
to and.
** * *
An easy one, a very easy one, from apostle
M’Neill : “Do you think that I would go on,
night after night and week after week —very
likely shortening my days with the incessant
strain—if I thought there was nothing in it?”
No, M'Neill, emphatically no 1 There’s a lot in
it, beside the “satisfaction of doing good,” and
you can swear to that from the bottom of your
redeemed soul. There’s corn and oil and first-
class travelling, and, above all, the cheap notoriety
that the aforesaid soul hankers after, in it; other-
wise, old man, you would be shortening your days
among the squalid millions of London or New
York, instead of rushing round the world as at
present. You could give the same old gospel to
more ignorant people, in much less time and under
much more Christ like conditions, if you chose to
settle down in the Mile End Road, but you enjoy
newspaper reports and pious “receptions,” and
hysteria, and other etceteras that gratify your
rabid reverend passion. There is a lot in your
game for you, John M'Neill, except just when a
bigger showman crosses your track and knocks the
glory out of you pro tern. What is a M'Neill
beside a Talmage, who positively charges for
admission ?

“ H.,” to Tub Bulletin, from Melbourne; “The Second
Mrs. Tanqueray,” strikingly able as it is, will not live, in
my opinion, either as an acting play, or as pure litera-
ture. The grey hairs are apparent now, in representa-
tion, and because this drama is essentially one of
surprises. The audience has coarse-minded people in a
majority. They know the plot and characters before-
hand, and swear over all the delicate situations with
vulgar gaffaws. The play is too real. It may, in this
aspect, be compared to such an inferior work as the
extinct “Drink,” Warner’s Zola-Reade piece. All the
great dramas that claw the stage are artificial, nothing of
a photographic cast. Look at " Camille.” The unreality
saves it, makes it a work of art. Duse’s Camille is quite
angelic. Mrs. Tanqueray is a brutal transcript. Perhaps
it is not a fault, but it is a fact, that sympathy with her is
only roused in the last scene. However, powerful is not
a strong enough word for the play—as a galvanic novelty.
It left me as though skewered with a sword, all night.
Dear Bulletin, —Henry Irving has not yet been pre-
sented at Court. The great event mysteriously lapsed at
the eleventh hour, and the Royal Drawing-room floor was
not bestridden by England’s only tragedian, after all.
The answer to your question why actors are more royally
favoured than actresses, may not be far to seek.' A
woman is on the throne. —B.E.
Chief Justicess Madden’s waxworks have been the latest
Melb. big go, at the Alexandra. It was Tennyson’s
“[Dream of Fair Women.” There they lay, in the tableau
entitled, “ Beauties of the Harem,” after which came Dr.
O’Hara’s song of the Holy City, “Take me there, take
me there!” Unfortunately, the cast was not given, or
the chronicles of glory might immortalise the fair Zuleika,
the peerless Zobeide, the sweet Haidee, and the all too
toothsome Qulnare.
Mrs. Needle is Melbourne wax-worked. Perhaps fairer
play than that of the bloodthirsty newspapers.
Paradis: “ Melbourne will not have the society drama.
Going to the amphitheatre of the Princess the third night
of ‘Mrs. Tanqueray,’ 1 found the curtain rise on the
house only three-quarters full, and dress-circle not good.
After the thud-almost—of ‘Captain Swift,’a fine play
by an Australian, Williamson and Musgrove have allowed
society drama to go by the run, and it is remarkable they
did not attempt to lift even ‘Mrs. Tanqueray.’ No, the
Adelphi drama is safer, as a rule, or farcical comedy.”
Histrion: “ I think ‘Randolph the Reckless’ a bit hard
on the Melb. footballing mummers. None of them went
in because they liked it, and they acted quite unselfishly
for the fund. Whatever may be said about the public
rush, surely it is a noble thing, and an example to all
other professions, for the Thespies thus to stick together.’’
The Melbourneites think “Mrs. Tanqueray" is “Pink
Dominoes.” When Oapt. Ardale says to Eileen, Tan-
queray’s daughter, “Come into the garden for five
minutes,” there is a prodigious guffaw.
Belle Cole and Co. gave four concerts at Hobart Exhi-
bition Hall, the last one, a shilling all over the house, to
an audience of about 3000. This, contrasted with the
poor success of the Sapio-Urso people in their Tasmanian
season, is a striking argument in favour of popular prices.
The latter Co. insisted on tip-top prices (45., 3s. and Is.)
despite friendly advice, and came out on the wrong side
accordingly.
“ The Second Mrs. Tanqueray” has duly shocked the
holy folks of Smellbourne. The Argus and Age are both
horrified at such immorality. The latter, in a notice of
stupendously ungrammatical silliness, says the play is
“ unpleasant ’’—stupid word. The Argus is most in-
dignant that such a wicked play should have soiled the
boards of a theatre in moral Melbourne. What exquisitely
crazy hypocrisy 1 The more so after the papers have
simply blubbered in delirious superlatives over the opera,
“ Ma Mie Rosette,” the plot of which is deliberately sug-
gestive.
Sraythe gives the antidote before the bane; Talmage
before Besant.
A baby, whose coming caused unusual commotion in a
well-known theatrical co., recently had a birthday, when
papa took the little darling behind the scenes and exhi-
bited her with exceeding pride.
The musicians in Melb. don’t do so badly, compared
with other show-people. For one thing, they stick so
well together, with their trade society. When the last
Montague-Turnerseason was dying out, at the Bijou, with
fearful waits of the drop curtain, the shiverers bn the
beggarly arrays whispered, “Tne band is making them
cash up.” How is it that actors have none of the
musicianly glue ?
Little Alma Grey, the clever four-year-old song-and-
dance artist of Melb. Alhambra, is the daughter of Alf.
Boothman.
In one Melb. theatre salaries are paid to the stage-
hands (extras and supers., not the artists) on Siturday
nights after the performance—the idea evidently being
that Sunday is a good day for sobering up.
Mr. J. P. Macdonald has partly recovered from his long
illness and began, last week, to put in a rather ghost-
like appearance in the front of Her Majesty’s (Sydney).
His numerous and enthusiastic friends are hereby warned,
however, not to touch the exhibit, which, though con-
valescent, is not fully recovered, and can’t stand too
much obtrusive cordiality just yet.
At “ Chief-Justicess Madden’s Grand Entertainment,”
n Melb., Madame Pinschoff (Elise Wiedermann) was hilled
to sing, but she did not, and hei place was taken—without
announcement—by a feeble amateur who could not secure
a recall even from a house packed with friends. Next
day Argus, Age and Herald had flattering comments on
Madame Pinschoff’s singing. In future, that lady is likely
to be as decided in her opinion on criticisms as her friend
Marshall Hall, who says she is the only vocalist in Mel-
bourne.
Arthur Leslie Boucicault, whose death in Sydney has
just been chronicled, has a daughter now in England
studying for the musical profession. Mary Boucicault
has a nice soprano and is the youngest of the three
daughters. The two elder ones marrned the Nuttings,
sons of Judge Nutting, and both are now in England.
Next month Lily De Alba, wife of the Italian opera
singer, proceeds against the Freehold Investment Co. for
£20,000 damages sustained in the lift accident. Madame
has never recovered from her terrible injuries.
Evangelist M'Neill continues to boom in Melbourne and
his success is likely to interfere with the Talmage show.
The voluntary collection one day this week amounted to
over £BO, a result as much due to Burke’s good singing
as to M'Neill’s rugged eloquence. High-churchman Rev.
Horace Tucker has organised an opposition show at South
Yarra, where the Oxford M.A. in surplice parades the
streets at the head of the choir.
It is the last straw that gives the camel
the hump.
Yic. Treasurer Carter and his pals quibble
recklessly about their consideration for the low-
paid men in Government employ. In his Budget
Carter cried aloud that the men at the bottom
were not touched, but this is only technically true
in re the public service, exclusive of railways. In
the railways, men getting 4| days’ work per week,
at 7s. per day, are retrenched at the rate of 5 per
cent., which is increased to 7 per cent. Five per
cent, off 31s. 6d. per week is a barbarism to which
even the immoral trams would not stoop. But
Patterson does it, with the gash in his face that
Melbourne papers call a smile.
Angus Cameron, M.L.A., who professes
to be a most enthusiastic opponent of payment of
members, and rode into N.S.W. Assembly on
that ticket, claims the Chairmanship of Com-
mittees (salary .£BOO a-year) as a matter of right.
Angus threw up his little Govt, billet to re-enter
Parliament, and as he opposes payment of mem-
bers, and yet can’t live on air, it would appear
that he had the Chairmanship in view all along.
In fact, it was generally understood long before
the election that if he and the Reid Government
both got in he was to have the billet. Therefore,
it would seem that Mr. Cameron, regarding
his own “screw” as safe, was merely ob-
jecting to the other people’s “ screws.” Now, the
Assembly ought to help him to live up to his
principles by either making the Chairmanship an
honorary billet, or by appointing some one else ;
or if it does neither Angus ought to live up to his
own principles by nobly repudiating the £lBOO,
and should refuse to put that amount in his hinder
pocket to steal away his character. Angus, by
the way, says that Reid thrice pressed him to take
the Chairmanship, while Reid says that the
Ministry hasn’t yet given “the slightest considera-
tion ”to the subject. This looks almost as if
Mr. Cameron had been pressing himself to take
the job, and mistook himself for Premier Reid in
the dark. Such mistakes do happen.
A short time ago The Bulletin affirmed
that Dibbs was bad, but Parkes was a thousand
times worse. It now recalls that statement and
affirms that both are a thousand times worse.
During the few days between the General Election
and his resignation of office the ex-Republi-
can ex-Premier was busily engaged in providing
for his friends and hangers-on. Light on these
various little jobs is being gradually afforded, and
it now transpires that Arthur Galton, Gov. Duff’s
Private Secretary (who has just resigned), has
been appointed at a high salary to complete the
“Official History of New South Wales,” which
sits like a nightmare on the chest of the suffering
province. Galton did not get along well with Duff.
He made himself useful to Dibbs; Dibbs would cut
off his right hand to spite Duff since the squabble
over the Council nominations—and there you are.
Dibbs, who owes his means of livelihood, his social
position, even his title, to the votes of the people
who believed his protestations and put him into
power, entrusts a national literary undertaking to
an academical person who has been a little over
a year in Australia, while there are at least a
dozen men in the province thoroughly qualified by
ability, by experience, and by public service,
to do the necessary work. This is what Australia
has come to after a hundred years of life and
growth ; she is openly told that for the writing of
her national history she must have recourse to the
recently-imported figurehead. Dibbs spits in the
face of Australian democracy, and a Guvment’Ouse
flunkey spurns us with his heel. It makes The
Bulletin very sick.

Justice Stephen, who is regarded as one of
the most religious of the N.S. Wales Judges,
omits the prayer at the end of the death-sentence.
The Earl of Kiutore, Governor of S.A.,
was born on 12th August, 1852. He will probably
celebrate hia next birthday as Governor of Vic-
toria.
Apropos the Needle (Melbourne) arsenic-
poisoning case. Of the “ leading English
arsenical poisoning cases is one in which the con-
victed woman was named Cotton.
Mrs. De Pentheny O'Kelly, daughter of
N.S.W. Judge Forbes, has “arranged” for the
publication in London of a novel, presumably
Australian, called “A Wicked Woman.”
Edward John Eyre, the Australian ex-
Slorer who lost popularity with the whites of
amaica for too great justice to their black
brethren, will be 79 on Wednesday s August 15.
Mrs. Morris, n4e Miss Wilmot, to whose
oeauty The Bulletin made allusion last week,
was one of the five Victorians in the “Types of

of ’92.
Brokenhillionaire M ‘Culloch is building a
place in Queen’s Gate, London, which will make
a hole in £50,000. There is to be a picture-gallery
of large proportions, and an organ is being
specially built for him.
G. B. Barton’s magnum opus , “ The Life
of Wentworth,” will be printed in Sydney
under the personal supervision of the author. It
is whispered that the book is to be turned out by
the Govt. Printing Office.
The long arm of coincidence ! A down-
South doctor, whose father had been one of
Kossuth’s officers in ’4B, called to see a patient
the other day, and found in the dining-room an
old man who had served in his father’s corps.
Ex-Minister “Dick” O’Connor’s health
has very much improved since he has been in
England. He is a general favourite (says The
Bulletin’s correspondent), and runs G. R.D. very
close in the estimation of the fair sex. He was
quite the lion of a recent garden-party at the
Botanic Gardens.
Old “ Bobbie ” Rand, of Mahonga Station,
Albury, who died, the other day, from the effects
of a cold caught during the Sydney wool-sales,
was one of the best known and oldest squatters.
He was 76 years of age and a confirmed bachelor,
and up to within two days of his death had never
had a day’s sickness. He had shorn over 250,000
sheep every year, and had as many as 64 shearing-
machines.
It is really true, despite denials, says The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, that Mount
Morganaire D’Arcy’s daughter is engaged to the
young Duke of Marlborough. This should make
Salmon Wilson squirm, for he could only trap an
impecunious earl, whereas the duke, besides the
bigger title, lias some money. The open-handed
D’Arey’s son, too, is making a good match —
money and “ birth combined.
Dr. Constance
Stone is the only one
of the eight medical
women practising in
Melb. who is not a
graduate of the local
University—her de-
grees are from Canada
and London. She
commenced two years
before the others, and
has a large practice.
Before medicine she
studied art, and
gained her livingby
teaching, and paint-
ing pictures. She is
now married to Dr,
Jones, who, besides
being a busy medical
man, is pastor of the
Welsh Church. Her
sister, Dr. Clara, was
governess before sue
was doctor.

begged and implored
Baronet Clarke not
to retire from Vic.
Legislative Council.
They prostrated
themselves before the
many-acred one, and
told him, in a bumble
petition, that if be
retired “ the repre-
sentation of the whole
colony would suffer.
As a fact, the gilded,
good-natured William
rarely attends the

when the people at
his big, dreary bar-
racks of a house bore
him too much and
prevent him _ from

Then he goes and

Dean Cowper, of Sydney, was the third
husband of bis wife recently deceased. The lady
bad married into three professions—naval, medical
and clerical. The dean’s father, Archdeacon
Cowper, was wedded thrice, and the octo-
genarian dean has been twice married. _
The Age solicitors thought they had dis-
covered a marriage relationship between Speight
and the Midland Railway potentate who reoom-
mended him to the Victorian Government. But
when Purves, Q. 0., broached the subject in cross-
examination he found that he was dealing with
another mare’s nest.
Hugh R. Reid, N.S.W. Premier G.H.’s
brother, in Melbourne, is a fiery, red-bearded,
heavy-jawed man—like Emile Zola dyed scarlet.
He it was who first brought Shiels into public
notice as a Freetrader. Shiels sees better now,
and Reid is sorry he ever influenced the young
man towards political affairs,
Agent-General Duncan Gillies, as limned
by an English paper: “ A short, amiable little
man, with a red face and a bald head. He is not
in the least proud.” No Victorian has reason to
be these days. An invitation to drink at a 3d. bar,
given by an utter stranger, would now be accepted
by nine out of ten Melb. men. The tenth would
ask for the threepence to buy bread with.
C. D. Whitcomb, Foreign Secretary of
Tonga, has been committed on a charge of
criminal libel upon Mr. Alexander Malcolm Camp-
bell in the form of a report to the Tongan Premier,
Mr. Whitcomb used to be secretary to Hori Grey,
and is a very able pressman. The articles entitled
“ The Great Pro-Consul,” published in The Buir
LETIN some three years ago were from his pen.
Parkes attributes all his present misfor-
tunes to one week’s bad health. In that week h«
expressed himself tired of leading Federation, and
young man Barton snatched the wand from his
hand and has been running away with it ever
since. In the same week he declared himself
tired of Freetrade leadership, and young man
Reid grabbed that wand, and now sticks to it.
Mannington Caffyn’s novel, “Miss Milno
and I ” —written in part at Manly, N.S.W. —has
reached its fourth edition on the “ Yellow Aster”
wave. As a bit of artistic work it is as crude as a
masterpiece of Fergus Hume, but it will always
have an interest for Australians in its realistic
sketch of Sydney people and officials, as they
appeared in the great and disgraceful smallpox
scare of a decade since.
Miss Michie, daughter of the Q.C. of that
name, who is now lecturing on economic subjects
in Melbourne, is not at all the severe fin de siecle
young woman one might expect. On the contrary,
she is graceful and pretty, with a curiously naive
uplifting of the eyebrows as she talks. Her views
on drainage and on woman’s rights may be pro-
nounced, but she is the most womanly of women,
and daintily well-dressed.
The rejected Alfred Allen’s most poignant
regret is that he has been denied the opportunity
of challenging the intelligence of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment on the mellifluous English of his amended
Wood and Coal Bill. He maintains that certain
clauses and sections of it read as classically as
passages of Milton’s “ Areopagitica,” and that the
whole measure is couched in diction stately
enough for declamation in a university lecture-
hall.
Brian England, ex-metnber of the Bland
Holt Co., is heir to a large estate in Kent, and
has gone ’Orae to be reconciled to his father after
a separation of years. England was formerly a
navy-lieutenant, and has since been a sheep-farmer
in Maoriland, a cab-driver in South Africa, a
stoker on a steamer, barman in Melbourne,
journalist and actor. His grandfathers, on both
his mother’s and his father’s side, were officers of
the British army at Waterloo; his father com-
manded a regiment in the Crimea, and is men-
tioned in Kinglake’s big history.
Dr. Neild (the literary anatomist, who
sometimes, when he sits down to write, hardly
knows whether he holds in his band the pen or
the knife) has a wonderful collection of children’s
stomachs, which he has been gathering during 28
years as a vost-mortem expert. He has stomachs
of children of all ages—from before birth up to
five or six years—and keeps them as treasures in a
cupboard, along with his valuable first-edition
copies of precious books.
George Walstab, of Melb. Hbkald, was at
school (Merchant Taylors’, London) with the pre-
sent Bishop of Ballarat. One day, in conversa-
tion, Walstab mentioned the circumstance to
Thornton. “ Ah,” said the big-browed prelate,
“I remember you now—there were two of you,
brothers—George and Harry; George was always
getting into scrapes, and Harry wasn’t.” “ Yes,”
replied the old pressman, “ and there were two of
you also —Sam and Harry ; Sam was a prig, and
Harry wasn’t,”

Douglas Sladen, says The Bulletin’s
correspondent, is about the biggest lion-hunter in
England. At a recent at-home at his flat one
might have rubbed shoulders with celebrities who
are seldom captured by anybody else. The reason
his parties are so frequented by men, is that he
won’t invite any but pretty women, and he intro-
duces people to each other, a most agreeable and
therefore quite un-English practice. The Sladen
is now allowing his hair to grow long, and is quite
Fabian-looking, to say nothing of artistic.
James Shaw has secured a 24th share in
the famous Londonderry and is nominated as the
first Mayor of Coolgardie. Shaw, ex-Mayor of
Adelaide, is one of the luckiest men in Australia.
He first made a pile out of the building of that
costly structure, S.A, Parliament House, and
then struck oil at Broken Hill. His chief virtue
is his gorgeous, all-round liberality; champagne
flows like water at his banquets, which are given
on the slightest provocation. He was Mayor
of Adelaide for three years, spent about =210,000
a year one way or another, and finished up
nearly stone-broke.
“Noosey Bob,” the N.S.W. man-butcher,
was being questioned, recently, by the manager
of a Sydney brewery on some particulars con-
cerning his lawful trade. “Now, for instance,
what drop,” asked the manager, “would you give
a man of my weight and build?” “Well,
squeaked the noseless hangman, as he surveyed
his supposed victim with a calculating eye, “I
should say that about seven feet would do nicely,
Mr. Manager. Yes, my God’’—warming to his
subject quite enthusiastically, and his little eyes
glistening—“seven feet! —and there wouldn’t be
a kick left in you, sir !”
Governor Duff is about the most unfortu*
nate and left-handed sort of Governor who has
ever struck Australia. Woe is stamped on
him and his belongings. The old man
offends the Scotchmen on New Year's Day—the
Southern Irish on the 12th July—the Frenchmen
because he didn’t wear the right clothes going to
a Requiem Mass. He hopelessly offends ex-
Premier Parkes, who politically ravens for his
blood, and he fights with ex-Premier Dibbs who
longs to pack him ’Ome. Up to date, Premier
Reid is the only person whom he has not offended.
The Duff mozzle is out, and the Duff mana is
gone. The potato is cooked.
Ex-M.L.A. Dickens professes to be more
than gratified at the result of the recent N.S.W.
elections, alleging that he will now be able to give
fuller attention to his private business, and adding
that, after all, his talents are more practical than
speculative or theoretical. It would thus appear
that Mr. Dickens falls fairly in with the tenor of
(he speech delivered at a bucolic banquet a year
ago in which the orator remarked: “Ye haven’t
the imagination, Mr, Dickens, of yer father, the
author of ‘Dombey and Son’ and ‘Little Dorrit,’
but when it comes to buying or selling stock or
judging a bit of good grazing ground ye show a
practical power that makes ye twice as valuable.
When evangelist M‘Neill was encouraging
a Melbourne audience to name any relatives or
friends who wanted praying for, a tallow-faced
saint on the front seats cried, “ Pray for my only
brother!” A dark, gruesome woman followed
this lead (the tallowy person was probably a decoy
lamb) by naming her “ only sister ” as a suitable
subject for rescue. Then a cheerful voice from
the rear shouted, “Pray for my only father 1”
M‘Neill hasn’t tried this disgusting gag since the
child of one father got off his joke. Stick the
needle of ridicule into a show
and the gas goes out of it. The M‘Neill bladder
is absurdly thin.
“As soon as you get outside my pad-
dock,” said the genial squatter, as the travellers
were making an early morning start, “ turn to the.
right, go three miles along the road, and you’ll
come to the next stopping-place. If you hurry
you’ll get there in time for a late breakfast.”
On they went, and about twelve o’clock, finding
no sign of the road, they determined to stop and
boil the billy. Then on they went again till dusk,
when they camped for the night. About eleven
o’clock next day they found themselves—just out-
side the paddock. The “ paddock ” was 45 miles
by 40.

Parkes has now only one open barracker
in the N.S.W. press. But he is a powerful
barracker, as may be judged from the following,
which the other day appeared in the Fergusson
organ :
Bill Heyman is a J.P. at Mopunga, but he is better
known as a bankrupt butcher, and the rankest and
rottenest pretender to religion that ever disgraced the
most narrow-minded sectarian profession of faith that has,
in the history of human error and self-sufficiency, dis-
graced the Christian faith as taught by the man Christ
Jesus. We have denounced this “moral sarcophagus
whited with paint” as a disgrace to Mopunga as a man.
and a foul blot upon her escutcheon as magistrate, until
we are heartily sick of the subject; and would gladly give
this sink of moral nastiness an opportunity of obliterating
himself in a spell of destructive fermentation. But this
moral cancer is obtrusive. He talked at us in a railway
carriage last Wednesday, when we had just risen from a
bed of prolonged suffering ; and were so prostrated with
weakness, that we were unable to tell the whole car-full
of passengers what we desire them to know about Bill
Heyman. Alderman Zachary O’Hara will remember the
circumstance. In that cheerful manner of his, which is
in such exquisite keeping with his libidinous wink, and
the wide expansiveness of his belly-band, he expressed a
sniggering wonder as to what the editor of the Times
would do if we had not him to gird at weekly.
And so on. With such an advocate on tap there
is still hope for H. Parkes.

E.F.
Wasted Money.
It is simply wonderful in an enlightened age like
the present, how people will waste their money on
useless medicines. There are thousands of suf-
ferers in this country from liver torpidity, indi-
gestion, kidney complaint, and biliousness, who
pay half-crowns for so-called “ cures,” when they
could get far better and safer results from half an
ounce of Epsom salts, costing one half-penny.
Of course, we know this chemical will not give
permanent results—neither will any of the many
advertised nostrums—but it will give temporary
relief and not injure the system, whereas the
disguised drastic purging mixtures sold by
many quacks will cause many serious diseases.
Clements Tonic does not cure or relieve disease by
purging. It is a natural tonic restorative, and
acts by stimulating the digestive and secretive
apparata to perform their proper functions. It
rebuilds wasted nerve, bone and muscular tissue,
restores strength to the debilitated system, excites
and permanently strengthens the power of con-
cupescence, and ensures a long, happy, and
enjoyable life, as proved by the experience of Mr.
Francis Barnes, Goulburn, N.S.W., who writes
on May 25, 1893: —For about the last twelve
years I have been suffering from a derangement
of the kidneys. The symptoms from which I have
suffered were pains across the loins extending up
the spine. In getting up in the morning I felt
so bad that I could hardly lace my boots, owing
to the severe pains from which I suffered in the
back. Hearing so much about Clements Tonic
and the benefit sufferers had derived from it for
the above complaint, I gave it a trial, and before
I had finished the tenth bottle, I am happy to say
I was entirely cured. My case is not the only one
that has come under my notice, as some of my
relatives and friends have derived great benefit
from Clements Tonic; lam only too happy to
certify to the above. Yours gratefully, Francis
Barnes, Goulburn, N.S.W. '


A Great Deal of Nonsense
has been written —and believed, about Blood
Purifiers.
WHAT
PURIFIES THE BLOOD?
THE KIDNEYS
PURIFY THE BLOOD !
and they alone.
If diseased, however, they cannot, and the blood
continually becomes more impure. Every drop of
blood in the body goes through the Kidneys, the
sewers of the system, every three minutes, night
and day, while life endures.
WARNER’S SAFE CURE
puts the Kidneys in perfect health and nature
does the rest.
The heavy, dragged-out feeling, the bilious
attacks, headaches, nervous unrest, fickle appetite,
all caused by poisoned blood, will disappear when
the Kidneys properly perforin their functions.
There is no doubt about this. Thousands have
so testified. The theory is right, the cure is right,
and health follows as a natural sequence.
Be self-convinced through personal proof.

The appointment of Jacob Garrard as
N> g.W. Education Minister is a grave joke.
When N.S.W. Premier Reid goes up for
re-election somebody must ask him whether he
w ill take a title if it is offered to him.
Downer has discounted his chances as
S.A. Tory leader by his pronouncement in re con-
fiscating property after the owner’s death.
Melbournian : I have broached my new
idea of removing the capital to a Gollins-street
land-agent M.P. Says he, “For Heaven’s sake,
don’t— you’ll be killed.”
Argus on the Vic. Upper House; —“So
long as the criticism is honest, as we are sure all
the criticism of the Council will be.” As it will be
where honesty is the best policy.
The area of the bit of territory in dispute
between Victoria and S.A. is 400,000 acres, valued
at £2 an acre, but probably S. A. would gladly
t ike a quarter of the sum and cry quits.
The new N.S.W. Ministry contains four
I wyers, one butcher, one produce agent, one
mechanical engineer, one coal-miner, one grazier,
one ex-railway clerk and present land-auctioneer.
The now-rejected Mr. Dowell was, if The
BjILETIN mistakes not, the member who intro-
duced to N.S.W. Assembly Abigail’s Banking
Bill, and attempted to pilot through the Colo
Valley Grab Bill.
The writing on the wall in Queen’s Hall
Parliament House, Vic., is: “In the multitude
of councillors there is safety.” Yet there is a
strong desire in Victoria to reduce the multitude
by about one-third.
The Vic. Customs yielded £34,000 less
than the estimate for the past year in sugar
duties. What a hopeful time is this for the bogus
beet-sugar industry, which will soon be absorbing
Patterson’s £lO,OOO bonus !
The final adjustment of Chicago Exhibition
accounts shows that N.S. Wales spent in that
meteoric project £28,924. The net tangible asset
is one happy little bebaubled doctor —“who
stands so high ” —that’s all.
The Maoriland Credit Fonder scheme,
which Sydney Telegraph denounced as contrary
to all good Freetrade principles, is cordially
endorsed by the London Times. Evidently the
Times is backsliding into Protection.

Dear Bulletin, —“Jim” Toohey, who
polled 12 votes at Tamworth, with a committee
numbering 38, did far better than a candidate for
another electorate, who boasted to the writer that
he had, in addition to the solid Orange vote, a

Mr. Chanter, M.P., whose vote has been
mainly democratic, wishes it stated that he was
adopted by N.S.W. National Ass without his
knowledge or consent. So were a lot of other
people. The roughest thing on the Ass is that
not one politician in Australia dares openly
recognise said Ass as his godfather.
The one thing which the Patterson-Carter
crowd swore to do was to get the finances straight.
This was the beginning, middle and end of their
policy. And now they have made a worse muddle
of the public cash than even poor old Berry did.
A Ministry which can only do one thing and can’t
do even that has no excuse for existing.
The sentiment of temperance-township
Mildura (Vic.) on the question of establishing a
distillery has been declared by vote : 389 inhabit-
ants were for the distillery, whilst 89 opposed it.
Of the drinking majority, 283 voters favoured the
idea of municipal grog-shops on the Gothenburg
system, but 77 friends of private monopoly pleaded
for the wicked old pub. system, with its pimply-
faced capitalist in tow.
There is quite a probability that N.S.W.

filling an ad interim chair, may yet direct the
military to suppress a shearing strike and com-
mand “law and order” to be maintained in a
wharf - labourers’ emeute against blacklegs at
Circular Quay. Ho w M’Millan enjoys the fore-
taste of this political happiness !
The Maoriland House of Representatives
proposes to shut clown on voluble legislators by
limiting the speeches to ten minutes when the
House is in committee, and to half-an-hour on
other occasions. This allowance is quite enough
for a brilliant man, and a great deal too much for
the ordinary bore. Any man can say more in
half-an-hour than any other man can remember.
The latest scheme is a land-grant railway
connecting Port Darwin with Adelaide. Length
of line, 1100 miles; estimated area to be granted
to the grabsters, 88,000,000 acres, or a-fourth of
the entire Territory ! S.A. Assembly will, no
doubt, emphatically reject the proposal, despite
the tempting bait of the expenditure of nine or
ten million pounds of foreign capital on the line.
Dear Bulletin, —What’s the kink in B.
R. Wise? Is it his University training, his here-
ditary class instinct, the sin by which the angels
fell, the curse of Reuben, or is he Parkes-bitten ?
Perhaps a conglomeration of the lot. He poses as
the democrat, and land-taxer and reformer.
“Instead of which,” he considers Parkes the
“natural leader of the N.S.W. Liberal party !”
If Wise doesn’t toe the mark within the next
three months he is for ever damned as a serious
politician. —B. B. B.
Premier Kingston has issued a proclama-
tion which, in effect, assumes S.A. control over
the disputed strip of land now under Victorian
jurisdiction. The S.A. Premier is the first man
in 40 years to face the difficulty and Patterson
must now do something. S.A. lawyers assert
that S.A. has legally an unanswerable case. In
fact, N.S. Wales, years ago, settled a similar
trouble with S. A., conceding her claims, which, as
in this case, were as to locating the 141st meridian.
Victoria has dilly-dallied for 40 years notwith-
standing reams of correspondence with S.A.
Kingston now proposes to force a settlement, and
wishes the matter referred to the Australian
Chief Justices, neither province to be represented
by counsel.

candidate received two votes —his own
and his coachman’s. Close shave for
the coachman !

The Munro Government (Vic.) in 1892
raised the duty on imported woollens from 30 to
40 per cent., and as a consequence the bleeding
country paid only £76,000 in duty next year
against £121,000 under the lower tariff. Also,
during the first year of the high tariff, the price of
locally-manufactured woollen goods, which had
been steadily falling since 1876, reached the lowest
figure ever recorded in the province.

Mildura, Vic., has polled in favour of
locally establishing the Gothenburg licensing
system. The difference between the Gothenburg
system and prohibition is the difference between
good and bad liquor. Under prohibition you buy
the essence of eternal punishment from a hell-
keeper, and dispense with the law; under the
Gothenburg system you buy decent liquor that
lubricates the soul, dispensed by the law.

N.S.W. PREMIER REID—as he rinked a few seasons ago.
Caldwell’s (S.A.) emasculating Bill is a
step back towards savagery. If it is the
beast in one man that impels him to the
offences in question, it is no less the beast in the
other that impels him to requite brutality with
brutality. The “right-thinking person”—clamour-
ing for ferocious punishment of ferocious crimes—
thinks it’s the eternal fitness of things that’s got
into him, whereas it’s only the brute instinct of
retaliation, masquerading as a sense of justice.
Treasurer Carter’s 10 per cent, drag-net of
all and sundry unspecified articles has this to
recommend it; that it will prepare the way for
that “good time coining” when all imported
articles whatsoever will have to jump a barrier of
1000 per cent., and thus enable us to see if Aus-
tralia can really support its people. A country
necessarily dependent on any other for any of its
necessaries is bankrupt so far as nation-making
capabilities are concerned, however good a depot
for Mark-lane it may be.
The Sydney correspondent of the Age
points out that 150 candidates at the last N.S.W.
election would, under the old system, have for-
feited their £4O deposit, and, therefore, the
present Electoral Act has lost the country £6OO
in hard cash. By the same variety of logic, if the
£lOO poll-tax on each Chinese immigrant were
abolished, and 10,000 Chows arrived as the
immediate consequence, the Age person would
probably point out that the State had lost
£1,000,000 in one fatal act. Arithmetic, properly
applied, can prove a whole lot of things.
Sydney Daily Telegraph regrets Parkes
does not see that his day is passed. What short
memories are possessed by bobbers up and down
in the world of facts ! That is the paper which a
few years ago declared it had Parkes in his coffin
and that the duty of the country was to screw the
lid down tight without more ado ; and six months
afterwards it vehemently embraced the role of
putting Parkes on the pinnacle of power as being
the only man with the necessary intelligence and
influence for the post. Parkes knows that on the
morning his political horizon reddens, the entire
office will crawl around him on its knees.
The fallen Dibbs will be remembered chiefly
as the man who didn’t establish the Sinking Fund,
about which he boasted so inuch ; who made no
serious effort to enforce his Income Tax when it
was rejected by the Council; who didn’t dare to
establish a genuine system of Protection ; and who
didn’t abolish payment of members. Also, he will
be remembered, though rather less definitely, as
the man who didn’t pass that famous “compre-
hensive Banking Bill,” and who didn’t even get
started with his Local Government scheme. The
number of things which Dibbs didn’t do is almost
as remarkable as the number of things that Reid
is thinking about doing by-and-bye.
Messrs. Winter and Isaacs are introducing
a Distress for Rent Abolition Bill in Vic. Parlia-
ment. The bill is but the shadow of what it
ought to be, and only brings “democratic”
Victoria up to the level England reached years
ago—by exempting bedding and necessary wear-
ing apparel, to the value of £2O, from the grasp
of the landlord. Little Dr. Maloney has shown
true patriotism and self-sacrifice in allowing these
men to bring the bill in, knowing the temper of
the House against men of his stamp. He tried
for total abolition on previous occasions, but, of
coui'se, failed. How long will this detestable
class privilege, allowing any brute to take the law
into his own hands against the most helpless
people in the community, be allowed to endure?
Get rich, run up £lOO with your baker, and com-
pound for a farthing! Be poor, owe a week’s
rent, and it shall be accorded to you that Fat
Man shall come in and loot your life’s collection
of household gods!
The Geelong Salt Works job becomes more
hideous the more it is looked into. With Federa-
tion and Tnterprovincial Freetrade the manu-
facture of Victorian salt must come to an end,
anyway, inasmuch as the present duty of £1 on
solar salt from South Australia (where a warmer
climate assists the evaporation of sea water) is a
bare protection for the local article. To-day the
S.A. salt can be sold in Melbourne as cheaply as
that produced at Geelong, wherefore it is fair to
suppose that tropica! Bananaland may at any time
kill the trade in both the Southern provinces.
There is no future for the Geelong works. If the
Patterson Government persists in selling the fee
simple of 690 acres of coast for a “song,” the
secret of the transaction may presently reveal
itself in a substance very different to salt. Coal
has been suggested. If a deposit of coal lies under
these precious “salt” works, the new syndicate
will fix up a wharf and ship its sinful treasure
right from the pit’s mouth. Wanted, the names
of any Members of Parliament who expect to
benefit by passing this ugly Bill.

The impression is abroad that O’Sullivan,
M.P. (N.S.W.), is about to swallow himself and
return from his Victorian travels a good free-
trader.
South Australia winds up the year with
a surplus of .£llOO, and to realise this amount
Treasurer Holder frankly admits that he has gon 1
down on his stomach to look with a microscope
for every possible stray copper, and has scraped
them in so industriously that he doesn’t know
where he will find another. The economies of the
Kingston Government during the past year would
make interesting reading. It has saved money in
pens, ink, paper, and envelopes ; it has economised
in paint and varnish and door-mats and cab-fares,
kept an eye on the consumption of gas, and
stopped a penny a day off the policeman, and has
cut down military pageants, and put the coal on
short allowance, and crawled on its knees to look
for a postage-stamp. But it has come out solvent,
and its labours are rewarded. Now that the
borrowing curse has come home to roost in real
earnest, it is a hard and dreadful thing to be an
Australian Treasurer.

Some scientist or other avers that no man
can tell a lie with his hand shut. It instinctively
opens and gives him away. It is further declared
that no average man can tell a lie and keep his
feet or his mouth steady. Also actor-man Baker,
when he personates the lying witness in the
“ Woman’s Revenge,” at Sydney Majesty’s, goes
on the theory that no man can tell a series of
awful whoppers and keep his head steady. The
moral evidently is that the witness’s feet should
be fastened with ropes to the bars of the witness-
box, and his head secured by tying his hair to the
ceiling, and his hands fixed with plaster-of-Paris
so that he can’t open them, and a porous-pi as ter
should be put over his mouth, and then he should
give his evidence through his nose. Under these
conditions it is quite plain that he can’t he.

Premier Forrest’s visit to Coolgardio
appears to have been not quite so dignified an
episode as was suggested by press reports at the
time. Tiie rejoicings opened with a reception of
the political pilgrim by seventy Coolgardiaiis,
mostly unsober, who wobbled forth from the
township on camels. Arrived at the meeting-
point, the reception committee drew itself
up in barbaric array and prepared to hiccup a
great welcome, but at this critical moment some-
body cried “ Whooshla 1 ” which, being inter-
preted, means “Camels, lie down 1” There-
upon the seventy ships of the desert dropped
into one unanimous attitude of prayer, and the
seventy riders rolled off and bit the dust, and
Forrest was received like a conqueror gazing at
the heaps of slain.
A similar lack of Vere de Vere repose charac-
terised the evening banquet. The brightest gem
in this crowning effort was a capacious oyster-pie,
built regardless of expense. After the toast of the
evening had been drunk 14 times without a pause
a local luminary (formerly of Broken Hill and
Sydney) mounted the festive board and danced a
digger’s hornpipe in the pie. Then he travelled
calmly up and down the banquet, dropping
oysters and undercrust from his savoury boots,
until another gentleman joined him. A joyous
destruction of crockery and glassware ensued, and
when Premier Forrest arose to return thanks for
Parliament he observed, with deep regret, that
many members of the representative assemblage
were sitting upon Uanc-mange. Having ex-
pressed a proper sense of wonderment at what he
had seen that day, the knight thanked his enter-
tainers from the bottom of his heart and with-
drew, leaving the bulk of the company to disperse
under the table.

“ Mrs. Chief Justice ” Madden’s “ Grand
Tableau Entertainment,” at Melb. Alexandra,
was stage-managed by a mixed committee, with
the Chief Justice in the chair. On the Saturday
previous to this superfine production “ Beau ”
Madden cast his eye over an afternoon rehearsal
of the tableaux from the centre of the dress-circle.
Mrs. Madden, rampaging about the stage with
Authority stamped upon her brow, would fix up a
group, and then one of the young ladies would
appeal for instructions to “ Sir John ” in the dis-
tance. Whereupon the great Judge usually
placed his head askew, and fired off suggestions
“a little more this way,” &c., until at last the
pictchaw pleased the august critic, and a chorus of
Toorakian belles cried, “ Sir John thinks this will
do nicely.” Nothing on the boards was half as
picturesque as the tableaux of Madden in the
dress-circle, nevertheless. Sometimes he grouped
himself as Apollo fondling a waxed moustache, at
other times he dimly suggested the Coronation of
George the Fourth, and, again, he would become
a reminiscence of plain J.M. filling the position
of referee at a scrapping-match. If they gave
rehearsals, instead of a settled entertainment, and
invited the public to come and see Madden bossing
the show, the privilege of paying to go in woulc,
be reckoned cheap at any price.
Mrs. Madden and cronies disposed of 1800 five
shilling tickets for the “charity” tableaux at
Melb. Alexandra.

A N.S.W. Instruction Department regula-
lion decrees that no inspector shall have under
him, in his school-district, any of his relations.
Unfortunately, the ediobis, at present, mostly a
dead-letter. Teachers view the aspect of an in-
spector examining any school presided Over by a
relative of his with the same feeling as the out-
side public do that of a Judge whose relations
defend or plead in a case upon which he is to
adjudicate.

The statue of Queen Victoria, given to
Adelaide by brewer Smith, was placed in position
by most irreverent workmen. Her Gracious was
hauled up by one rope round her neck and another
about her waist, while at one period of the pro-
ceedings her feet pointed straight to the noonday
sun. Seldom before has Majesty occupied so
many undignified positions in so short a time.

A well-advertised Sydney clergyman and a
dozen other enthusiasses hold a spiritualistic
seance every Friday evening. The individual who
goes into the trance state, a well-known footballer,
is supposed to be controlled by a Red Indian chief,
and utters enough guttural gibberish to satisfy
all demands. So keen has the interest in the
matter become, that the services of a shorthand
writer were engaged to take down as nearly as
possible the sounds as they were uttered, and his
report has been submitted to Col. Bell, Murkan
Consul, who has consented to ascertain if the
dialect is genu-ine. The subject is an Australian,
and has never travelled abroad, and is, so it is
said, as much interested as the rest, in finding
out the genuineness or otherwise of his spook-
con trok
* # *
N.S.W. Animals Protection Association
has dispensed with its inspectors for want of funds.
This means a temporary go-as-you-please among
the brute creation. The prevention of cruelty to
animals should be a State office as much as the
prevention of cruelty in other directions, and
should be no more dependent on the condition of
private funds.
« • •
Professor Tucker, of the curious coun-
tenance, was a witness for the Vic. Customs
Department in its attempt to prove a criminal
offence against Ovid’s “ Art of Love.” Tucker
considered the book indecent, but Dr. Bride, the
Melb. Public Librarian, and others were disposed
to regard it as a valuable classic which they would
not care to recommend to “the young person.”
So old Ovid is still to be sold in virtuous Victoria,
with the new recommendation that it is suitable
for gentlemen only. Let a girl distinctly under-
stand that a particular book is not good for her,
and she will never, never wish to read it.

Parson Talmage’s “ tabernacle ” was
burned to ashes shortly before he left New York ;
cause of fire unknown. One of his trustees now
explains how the combustion came about: —
“ That fire,” said Mr. Moody earnestly, “ was a provi-
dential dispensation. I think it was Christ’s way of help-
ing ns. He certainly did help us, I think. After I got
in the board we paid all our current obligations. We got
the Sage mortgage extended. We raised money by
popular appeal. We did all we could, and then Dr.
Talm »ge preached his farewell sermon. And then,” he
continued in a slow and solemn voice, 11 Christ said to us:
‘ Boys, you’ve done well. I'll relieve you of your
troubles.’ And then he burned the church.” As Mr.
Moody uttered these last words his voice sank almost to a
whisper, and his manner was intensely earnest. After a
pause of a minute Mr. Moody became more cheerful,
“ I don’t believe,” he said, “ that Christ believes in
running a church under a big debt. He called us to a
halt for that reason. I don’t believe Christ believes in
running a church with any debt. If he does, I don’t.”
That last sentence is evidently a serious warning
to his pal the Deity (“Boys,” &c.) that Moody
will only follow Him so long as He walks a chalk-
line. Moody feels that a church should be free from
debt, and if the Deity doesn’t feel likewise, He
ought to. If you want genuine, up-to-date blas-
phemy always apply to a shining light of the
church. *

A week or so ago they had a really
exciting half-hour in Carlton (Vic.)
police-court. A peaceful-lookirig elderly
lady got into the box to complain of
the eccentric conduct of one .James
M'Dougall, and a wild-eyed person
answering to that name was inserted
in the dock to answer the charge.
M'Dougall, according to the lady’s
account, used to trot behind her in the
street and call out “Stop thief ! ” and
she asked the court that this conduct
should be discontinued. Just here
defendant got hold of the Bible and
hove it at the head of an adjacent
official, and delivered an impassioned
harangue which broke up the gravity
of the court. Then he started to sing
“God Save the Queen ” and to undress
himself in court, but the police rushed
him ere he got all his clothes off and
held on the balance of his wardrobe by
main force. Meanwhile, prosecutrix,
fearing the spectacle of an absolutely
naked defendant, had bolted and left
the case unfinished. Consequently,
there was a difficulty in deciding what
to do with the accused, but at last the
bench fined him ss. on general prin-
ciples, and gave him six hours’ gaol
for undressing himself in the sacred
halls of justice.

Evangelist M‘Neill’s babbles
mainly to bald-headed men at his mid-
day services in Melbourne Town Hall.
He is the bringer of glad tidings to
busted syndicators and insolvent
church - goers of all denominations.
They may be seen in the body of the
ball, beaming upon the smug* “jolly”
man, with the wiry dissenting beard,
who tells them to believe and be
saved. That’s the religion for Jones,
of Collins-st., and Bouncer, of Flinders
Lane. No nonsense about high prin-
ciple, and discipline of the dmind, an
all that moral philosophy stuff. “Sim-
ple faith ” is the commercial bald-head’s
“ dart.’’ It is easier to accept God
than to believe in banks, so after singing
the Doxology and showing themselves
out at the door, they walk briskly off,
with peace in their hearts, and an in-
tensified contempt for irreligious
honesty and fair dealing. They have
been “ strengthened ” by the stuff that
makes any decent worldling sick. The
business person who can taste M‘Neill’s
hogwash without vomiting is to be
regarded with much suspicion.
The idea ! When a Melb. bookseller was
prosecuted the other day for selling Ovid’s “ Art
of Love,” an alleged wicked book, three witnesses
—including James Smith, of the Argus, and two
M. A.’s—got into the box and swore that Ovid isn’t
a bit more immoral than some parts of the Bible and
Shakespeare. Now, when Josh. Symes says that
sort of thing on a freethought platform it is
blasphemy. In a court of law it is a literary
opinion.
TO PHYLLIS: AN ARSENIC EATER.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Sweet Phyllis, I don’t really care
That arsenic ’tis that makes you fair;
Far, far such trifles I’m above,
For I am also drugged—with love.
Ah, gay coquette ! your world, though wide,
Is not enough to suit your pride—
“ More moths,” you sigh with every breath :
And so you needs must flirt with Death.
Death, captivated by your charms,
Would fain embrace you in his arms,
The grim old spectre smites, but lo !
You are the fairer for the blow.
I hold you in my eyes, sweet maid,
Your head with coiled, golden braid,
Your lithe form like a triune Grace,
The deadly beauty of your face.
Your sweet prescription ! would I were
The patient whose relief your bear ;
In truth, sweet Phyllis, I am sick
And need you—love and arsenic.
If you should scorn me," maiden fair,
Grant me, at least, one parting prayer;
In kissing you let me death seek
And sip oblivion from your cheek.
If we should marry, ah, sweetheart,
I ask one promise on your part:
Don’t let me, by sad writhings rude,
Find your complexion in the food.
Sydney.
ZEF.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad rticle for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you? Will you do your business at

• • •
Alderman Alexander Dean has the future
of Sydney boys greatly at heart, but as a builder
and contractor, and consequently a large employer
of raw apprentices, he grieves to confess that
Australian boys are not nearly so respectful and
reverent as are English and Scotch boys. Mr,
Dean, writing to a charity committee, the other
day, says that the remedy for this state of things
will lie in enforcing prayers and the reading of a
chapter of the Bible every morning. This may or
may not make boys submissive, but what if Mr.
Dean applied the medicine to his own apprentices
on the kerbstone in front of the next Australia
hotel he prepares to raise ?
* * •
Gordon Coutts, the Victorian artist, has
been permitted to paint a full-length portrait of
Mrs. £. £. Smith in all the glory of her Court
robes, just as she stood before Queen Victoria last
year, and with that vivacious look of being very
pleased to see her Majesty which the wife of so
world-renowned a scientist ought to wear. Gordon
Coutts has got all this into the lady’s faoer-and
more. He has painted the marvels of her dress in
such a way that women stand enviously and look
just as they look in some big shop window. It is
rumoured that the work has been offered as a pre-
sentation to the National Gallery.

Dear B ullbtin, —Wh ere lies th e superiority
of Chinamen over Europeans as husbands? Is it
not true that a European woman will rarely or
never marry a countryman after partaking of
wedded bliss with a Chow ? The latest instance
of this infatuation occurred in Hobart recently,
when a comely and clean-looking Englishwoman
married a Celestial four days after she had buried
her first husband, also a Chow. — Hobartian.

The fame of Oambooya (Q.) was recently
trumpeted over the world as a sanatorium (for con-
sumptives) without a peer. In the same week the
thermometer quietly crawled down to 10° below
freezing point. The population spent half the day
trying to thaw itself out, and half the weak chests in
the neighbourhood took permanent lodgings in the
local cemetery.

Dear Bulletin, —Your suggestion to the
mean man to carry half-sovereigns in lieu of
sovereigns is good. I know a Melbourne man
who carries a two-shilling piece. He asks you to
have a drink, planks down the coin, and when he
receives his Is. 6d. change demands another six-
pence —swears he put down a half-crown. In nine
eases out of ten it comes off. On one occasion he
got as change the same identical florin that he had
paid.—W.S.

Lawyer Gaunson has an interesting hypo-
thesis to advance on behalf of Mrs. Needle, the
alleged Melbourne poisoner. Most, if not all, of
the arsenicised relatives and friends of the lady
had been prescribed bismuth as a sedative, and
Gaunson, it is said, will produce evidence that
arsenic is not always foreign to bismuth prepara-
tions. .

“ Loyalty ” has come to a pretty pass in
Tasmania. Gov. Gonnanston attended one of
the Belle Cole concerts in Hobart, and when the
last but one item on the programme was over,
H.E. arose and departed, accompanied by Lady
G. and suite ; whereupon the conductor (he of the
local Exhibition choir) hastily substituted the
National Anthem for the song promised per pro-
gramme and thus concluded the concert.

Dear Bulletin, — “J.R.” says,
aroma of coloured persons,_ that the “ characteris-
tic smell” of the negro is not present in the
Asiatic. This is true, but the characteristic smell
of the Asiatic is very much like the other, and is
not in the least disagreeable when you get accus-
tomed to it. And again, your unnamed corres-
pondent, on trousered women, cannot have lived
in the Punjab. Providence does not give the
beautiful limbs of a beautiful woman to be hidden
by skirts or baggy trousers. A Punditanee, Pun-
jabee, or Cashmere woman can be more graceful in
skin-tight silk pants than most of the angularities
or adiposities in corsets, skirts, and other com-
binations. The most beautiful Asiatic woman I
ever saw wore yellow trousers. And your unknown
leaves with a good woman four things: Her
religion, her honour, her lover, and her petticoat.
My good woman, just named, had the first three,
the third being myself, and between us we had no
petticoat—only trousers. My friend. Dr. Brigham
Brown, knows more about women than the writer
you quote —Yours, Brighan Scotty Wrinkled.

Apropos of a recent Sydney case in which
an apparently dead child came to life on being put
in its coffin. A Sydney asylum contains a woman
who, many years back, revived just as she was
being screwed down in her coffin. The shock of
that gruesome awakening necessitated her removal
and confinement, ever since, in a mad-house.

Advt. from the matrimonial column of
London Standard of recent date : —
A DOPTION.-Americans and Others.—Will any
kind, rich Gentleman Adopt a Lady (34) and her
little Boy (7); in return for a quiet home and freedom,
from money troubles, she would be a faithful friend
and companion ; refined, gentle, good-looking,
accomplished, musical; an excellent and economical
housekeeper. Son, a dear little child, is most affec-
tionate and intelligent.—Address, R.S.V.P., care of
Library, 37 Southampton-row, W.C.
This tops the record, so far. “ And others ” is a
fine touch. It is mostly the others who “adopt ”
ladies of over 30 with “ freedom from money
troubles.”

Up to the end of June of this year the
S.A. Govt, had impartially distributed free to
applicants 141,379 trees and 149,915 vines. The
result in many districts of the province is a mar-
vellous transformation.
Dear Bulletin, —Here are the facts in re
the chief sources of Sydney poverty relief. There
is no systematic or united effort to prevent im-
position. The Labour Bureau only gives relief to
married people (the husband being registered on
the books as unemployed). Widows and widowers
are referred to the Benevolent Asylum; and
deserted wives likewise. Should a husband elect
to go fossicking through the Bureau, the wife is
not thrown on the Benevolent Asylum ; on the
contrary, she is rationed by the Bureau until such
time as he writes for the wife and family to be
sent up to him by the department, which has been
done in many hundreds of cases since “fossick-
ing” was started. At the same time, while draw-
ing from the Bureau, the wife may also be drawing
relief from the B.A. as well (the fault of the
system), having represented that the husband is
away looking for work, and omitting to mention
that he had been sent fossicking. When B.A.
finds the husband is out of work and in town it
certainly refers him to the Bureau on the chance of
its being able to place him on some job—and that’s
about all the reference that is made. The B.A.
has about 700 cases weekly and only one inspector
to enquire as to bona-fides, &c. The Bureau has
about 360 cases with four inspectors, who attend
to every ease once a week. What is wanted is
centralisation of relief, with all cases recorded and
classified for reference by subsidiary organisa-
tions. Where possible, relief should only be
given in exchange for labour. Mere charity
demoralises the recipient and increases the burden.
—Yours,
JESKI.
Bless you ! patriotic bart.,
Legislator sensible :
Whilst the country’s “ in the cart ”
You are indispensable
As the moon from oloudland dark
(Classic theme for penny verse),
So do you, O William Clarke,
Luminate the universe.
Had you carried out your throat,
Orator mellifluent,
’Twould have filled with mad r.'gret
Every constituent.
When you speak they whisper “ Hark !”• —
They were never critical —
“ Listen to Sir William Clarke 1”
Don’t he talk political!”
Few the things you had to tell
In a sense articular,
And your forte was doing—well—
Nothing in particular.
Still your mumbled crude remark,
Or your sentence blunderfull,
Coming from Sir William Clarke,
Sounded rather wonderful.
Therefore, patriotic bart.,
By your change of attitude
You have set a people’s heart
Pit-a-pat with gratitude.
Gay and joyous, like a lark
Loosened from a sparrow-net.
Toadies carol —“ Vote for Clarke 1
’Cos he is a baronet.’’
TO A BALLET-DANCER.
Diaphanous in tulle and gauze,
And lace, no doubt,
I must not look at you—because
Folks are about.
When on your toes you pirouette
And kick the “flies,”
I always use a friend’s lorgnette
To hide my eyes.
But for a lady’s hat I might
Drain pleasure’s dregs,
And now and then catch sinful sight
Of your—hem ! —legs.
But still, despite kind heaven’s care,
Which strikes me dumb,
I wish, although that hat is there,
I had not come.
My private thoughts a staid, black coat
Conceals from view,
It is much easier, I wote,
To see through you.
For, when your skirts go flutt’ring far
Up in the air,
I see not only what you are,
But what you wear !
Melb.
BERNARD ESPINASSE.
Beyond the Bars
(For the Bulletin)
“ Unto the poet's soaring soul
This earth is but a cage 1 ” he said ;
“ My spirit yet shitll reach its goal.
When this my feeble frame is dead.
I'll know tile secrets of the earth.
I’ll hold communion with the stars,
After my glorious glad new birth
Beyond the bars 1 ”
The barmaid thought that he was mad ;
She could not see the soul behind
Those glazed and drink-dimmed eves, yet sad
She felt for him who wished to find
Creation's secrets. So she spake
To one just stepped from off the cars—
“ May he be soon, for his own sake,
Beyond the bars ! ”
“ Only a wreck,” said he, “ who reeks
Of drink,” and turned and drank his fill;
Hi* goal was dross that commerce seeks,
His lore the wisdom of the till.
But that poor poet all unkempt,
He sought the loro that makes and mars.
For of high regions ho had dreamt
Beyond the bars.
The man and girl are underground,
Dead as the dirt that wraps their frames ;
No honour have their actions found ;
Forgotten even are their names.
The pu-t has his wish at last.
Fur Time has snatched him to the stars ;
His songs live on, though he has passed
Beyond the bars.
jMelb.

audience that filled the Town Hall at the Metro-
politan Liedertafel concert on the 2nd August by
