My Dear Moorabinda—
A huge Masonic ball, in aid of Masonic indi-
gence, came off very perkily at Sydney Town
Hall on 31st ult., altho’ the promised vice-regal
patronage wasn’t there, and the grand proces-
sion under crossed swords (rather reminiscent to
an unawed onlooker of the nursery game of
‘‘oranges and lemons ”) wasn’t headed by His
Ex. From a purely spectacular point of view,
the Governor’s defection didn’t amount to a row
of pins, for his worshipful substitute was a well-
known Sydney boniface of commanding presence,
in a gilded horse-collar and a jeweller’s shop-front
of signs and symbols, who evoluted to perfection,
and brought down the house. But a Governor
doesn’t strengthen his position by making pie-
crust of his promises at the eleventh hour; altho’
local ball-committees are getting used to the ting-
tong of the telephone at ten minutes to starting-
time, and a voice proclaiming in faint aide-de-
cainpish tones that His Excellency or Her Excel-
lency will not attend, having become indisposed
after dinner—no blame attached to the cook.
A rather depressing feature of the big Masonic
ball was that all the taking costumes seemed to
have got on to the ugly women, so I dare not
immortalise the garments that took my fancy •
there was a fair percentage of frocks that hit one
in the eye, as the French almost say. Imagine a
damsel in sanguinary scarlet even to her long
gloves—like Lonnen’s Mephisto. 1 Masonic in-
signia and jewels made the he-aspect of the floor
glitter like a pantomime-chorus, but the Nemesis
of Freemasonry seems to be that it is not until
brother Blank’s skull is sticking out of his hair
that he is eligible to “ wear a jewel on his head.”
The young and bewitching Mason is crudely orna-
mented, as a general rule, with a thing like an
empty handkerchief-sachet suspended where a
savage generally masses the whole of his ward-
robe, while his Masonic grandfather can’t move
for bullion and rheumatics.
A mummer, who sometimes arranges entertain-
ments for people who don’t know how, expressed
himself recently on the moral difference between
two leading Australian Govmentouses in the
matter of stage exhibitions. Only the other day
a charity programme was submitted to a high
official who, looking down the list, decided that a
ballet would liven things up, and asked the
manager if he could tuck one in any where. “ But,
of course, with no tights, if you please, Mr. '
Haresfoot—his Excellency don’t approve of ’em.”
On the disgusted actor-man saying that a ballet
without fleshings was impossible, the High Official
waived the point and said that perhaps a discreet
skirt-dance with opaque draperies might be
arranged instead. As it eventuated they had
neither leg-show nor peg-show on that eventful
evening—but as his Ex. stopped away just as
frigidly from the extremely Bowdlerised bill they
concocted in deference to his modesty as he might
have done if they had included Bartho in the
entertainment, the haggling went for nothing—
unless to advertise the fact that the most unpopular
Governor in Australia won’t look at Tottie Redlegs,
even through his fingers.
But at another and more palatial Guvmentouse
the showman who is commanded to get up an.
entertainment to amuse the Guv’nah simply has
the whole world to pick and choose from, and
never need shy at a clever turn because it is a little
cerulean. In fact, their Excellencies have sat out
one or two things that have even deepened to
indigo here and there, and yet nobody was
beheaded after the performance.
Five o’clock gossip has been drawing its inspira-
tion from the back-blocks recently, and everybody
has been tattling of the secret marriage of Mrs.
Pure Merino’s pretty governess with the coach-
man, who only had a promising pair of legs to
recommend him. As several more or less pro-
minent Sydney families are related to the well-
connected heroine, she is receiving much pity and
commiseration for her rash act, but I haven’t met
anybody yet who wasted any sympathy on the
bridegroom. And yet in the mating of white and
horny hands, I think the latter generally get the
worst of the deal in the lifelong sequel.
Sydney has its “chahut’’—practised by club
youths who go yelling along quiet streets at mid-
night-one man making a noise like a dog with
two tin kettles tied to his tail and, moreover,
keeping his aristqcratic eye out for any mischief
he may do in passing. But he is not always
allowed to hovl in peace. The other evening a
covey of youths, uttering their strange, weird cry
and kicking over the dirt-boxes as they progressed,
were making tracks for their club—a particularly
swell one. Imagine their surprise when they
were arrested on the step thereof and put firmly ,
into the lock-up for the night, no friend being'
available in the small hours to bail them out 1
They hoot pianissimo now.
Owing to unforeseen obstacles, I regret to say I
missed the At Home (dancing) given by the
students of Sb. Andrew’s College, the other Thurs-
day. _ By the way, as the college’s coat-of-arms is
a shield like a tea-tray with an open Bible flat-
tened out on it, a dance is the very last thing one
might expect from the collegians—unless the cards
went out in this wise : “To meet the daughter of
Herodias.”
That gifted actor, Mr. Sass, who looked on the
stage as if he had evoluted from a furled umbrella,
departed ’Ornewards last mail. Off the boards,
Mr. S. abandoned himself to botany and his
garden-Latin was a treat to listen to, although he
wasn t an infallible weed-christener by any means.
X remember hearing him discoursing of the rarity
and unusualness of a plant to which he gave a
blue blossom every ten years and a double-jointed
name and then looked round for applause. Where-
upon a still, small Australian voice said, un-
feelingly ; “Nonsense; it’s only a Queensland
pineapple plant!’’
The “Daffodil Dance” in aid of Sb, Francis’
Church happened in Paddington Town Hall on
2nd inst. in the presence of Mayor and Mayoress
Manning. The latter wore the most good-looking
gown in the room, and carried a well-built commit-
tee posey of daffodils and ferns. Whether it is an
actual kindness for a committee to handicap their
lady president with 71bs. or so of garden produce
from her entrance is open to controversy—but,
fi-i er a co,n pliment’a a compliment, even if
(like the handshake of the N.S.W. Sergeant-at-
Arms) it sprains one’s wrist. The Daffodil Dance
was a success of numbers; the wall trimming
(supervised by Mrs. Blankstone) was effectively
Irish' Had it not been for one or two unforgive-
able costumes and the bumpy music, the Daffodil
Dance would have shaped very well for an
eleemosynary outbreak.
Two more big public balls and then the season of
chanty hopscomes toan end. These final events are
the Boys’ Brigade ball early in Sept., and the
Hospital Saturday ball later in the month. By the
way, until women learn how to run transitory ball-
committees without having the Riot Act read over
them, I think they should be “sealed oysters’’ on
the suffrage question. Never a ball arises but
fc‘ ie i re i are wigs on the green over the preliminaries,
and the final resolutions are always arrived at in a
whirl of fur and_ feathers. At a recent meeting
on a forthcoming ball one old lady, who is
perpetually in evidence, grabbed a lot of books and
hurled them at the heads of her colleagues ! She
didn’t hit anything, of course, except the moral
that poor, weak woman will always carry her
point on the Aunt Sally principle— two shots
a penny, so to speak—when indignant Billingsgate
fails her.
Dr. Talmage (a “Murrikan” friend of mine
calls him a circus preacher) was interviewed in
Sydney the other day, and his replies were all
admiration-points. He likes his audiences. By
the way, I never met a showman yet who didn’t
like his audience when it was large ; I have always
found that responsive and ready audiences weigh by
the physical ton, not the intellectual oz. But what
interested me most was the goose-profiled D.D.’s-
gush about local femininity. “ One thing your
womanhood. It’s beautiful—right throughout
this city it’s beautiful! Your ladies of position
seem to be (he is a little guarded here, you per-
ceive !) more artless than any it has previously
been my happiness and good fortune to meet!”
And yet, when I was sitting under Dr. T., on.
the School of Scandal night, when well-dressed
and ever-curious Frivolity turned up and crowded
the dowdy nonconformist females (who generally
have a lien on itinerant sensations of this sort) 1
into the aisles, I detected as much swansdown-
povvder and perpetual blush of youth in the “art-
less ’’ visages turned up to the Doctor as I have'
ever seen at a ball. And even if he meant the
adjective metaphysically, I cannot curb my bound-
ing astonishment at the artlessness of a Sydney
woman, or any other woman for that matter,
where a man is concerned (and Dr. T. must look
at the world in the third person masculine). But.
it is certainly a waving ostrich-plume in Dr. Tal-
mage’s cap that he didn’t find out himself how
heavily our “artless ladies of position” powder
their noses !
The first production of “Paul Jones,” at the'
(it debuted at the old Royal), drew a
circle brimming over with artless ladies of position
, bent on sampling the beauteous Ryley’s successor,
Mr. Wallace Brownlow, who made "his first boh
to a Sydney audience as Paul Jones in suits of
clothes facsimiles of the luscious Marion Burton’s,,
■even to those historical pale lavender grey knickers.
—but what a falling-off was there, my country-
men ! They might have been chaff-bags on Mr. B.
for all the electricity there was in them.
Facially, our very latest baritone reminded me’
tormentingly of Florence Young. He is a
large and effective person, who talks like.
Renouf, hugs his stage-love in a Bowdlerised sort
of way that must be eminently gratifying to the'
i sometimes offish disposition ot Nellie Stewart, andl
who sings—but he was too ill to be criticised when
I heard him. Still, ‘he lacks the animal magnetism
of his predecessors ; neither are his eyes Byronio
nor his legs a poem. If you want my candid
opinion as to the probable effect of a long course
of Brownlow on Australian dress-circles, I must
say that I don’t think he will be worried with,
little pink notes beyond the ordinary fate of a
good-looking, upstanding he-actor. He is merre
the cut of the little tin god on wheels of a foot-
ball club than a boudoir deity. By the way, Mr.
Brownlow has a brother—Mr. Fred. Brownlow—
who fills a Govt, billet in Sydney, and is a light in
the Naval Brigade.
The feature of Nellie Stewart, Violet Varley,
and, of course, the noble army of chorus-girls was
the run of rouge to their physiognomies. Nellie
Stewart looked like a hopeless case of sun-burn,
and Violet Varley’s new fashion of painting a red
bar like a half-mask of stage blush across the
upper part of her pert countenance, will decimate'
the ranks of her admirers in front if she persists in
it. I think May Pollard sported the newest gown
—a sort of rainbow mingling with spasms of gold
insertion. The Stewart wore almost the same,
frocklets as of yore, if my memory serves me, and
danced her pas seal in Don Trocadero’s palace as
pokerishly as ever, too. The lovely and irresist-
ible Nellie deserves piles of superlatives over most
things she tackles, but the pleated skirt of the'
danseuse isn’t included, to my mind.
“ Paul Jones ” was perforated with more or less'
happy political allusions, and Lauri, who used to
barrack in the pantomime about “Freetrade with
uld Parkes up,” gave it out as his “ Paul Jones ”
opinion that Dibbs would return to office in two
years, a wild statement that was received with
'approving yells. By the way, I am told that one of
the newly-married Premier Reid’s first official acts
was to admit a perambulator into the Botanical
Gardens! Fellow feeling for the man at the
wheel, I suppose 1
And, talking of Mr. Reid, reminds me that he’
once wrote, a long time ago, in a lady’s confession-
book that his “idea of misery” was “Tight
boots.” Perhaps he had a dim, prophetic fore-
boding even then that Bibbs’ shoes wouldn’t
quite lit him.
The opening and shutting of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment on Tuesday morning, without the millinery
support of His Excellency, was mainly remarkable
for the garden-produce in the buttonholes of
M’s.L.A. One fat man sported a deep red rose,
and another fat newly-elected senator a large pink
camelia—defensive measures taken to tone down
their maiden blushes, I suppose. President
Lackey (lovely name for a man in a horse hair
wigand a point-lace tie !) should learn a new bow
against the real circus opening on the 28th inst.
when Gov. “Duff approaches the Chamber.” As
it is, President Lackey bobbing awkwardly to the
audience with one hand on his digestion fills me
with a horrible dread that he’s going to recite
“Casablanca!” By the way, the small attendance
of women who turned up on Tuesday shows that
plumes and hanky-panky, not the charms of
our spasmodic legislators, are the things that
attract the girls.
lam in receipt of the “Canada Waltz,” by J.
Edward Sykes, dedicated to Madame Kellermann,
and which I had the privilege of hearing the com-
poser play the other day. In a musician’s hand
the waltz is bold and tuneful a thoroughly
masculine composition which will well repay
study—but its technical difficulties make it a
sealed book to the noble army of strummers. And,
talking of waltzes, Miss Maude Fitz-Stubbs has
received an autograph letter from Gov. Robinson,
congratulating her on the artistic merits of the
“ Orlando.”
Society is afflicted with a new handshake almost
as uncordial as the kangaroo-grab. It is easily
engineered. You elevate your arm, and hook the
tips of your fingers lightly, but firmly, in the
finger-tips of your antagonist, who hooks you, too,
as if each were trying to make crochet of the other.
Then you separate by mutual consent, and the
erecting is over. Mind you, people who labour
under the impression that an honest man’s grip
ought to sprain the wrists of all his women-friends
may look obliquely at these finnickings, but, after
all, the less one squeezes and paws one’s fellow-
man the better for everyone’s glove-bills, and what
may be called the crochet-hook how-do has the
blessing of many light and tightly-gloved women.
ours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, August 7.
On dit that the young wife of a prominent
Sydney politician, who eschewed matrimony fur
long enough, is already giving him shrill counsel
to stick to his profession and let unprofitable
politics “ rip.” How hubby must regret those
long, lonely years when he had no sweet partner
to stimulate and encourage his flagging energies !
At one of Evangelist M‘Neill’s meetings a
steward was trying to pack late comers into seats
already comfortably filled. Everyone squeezed
into as small a space as possible, except the occu-
pants of one bench who gazed straight ahead
without moving a muscle. Presently a gigantic
female pushed her way up the aisle to the official,
who, acting under the Evangelist’s directions, led
her straight to the rebels and whispered, “ Get in
there, mum, if you can ! ” The lady accepted the
invitation, and as the choir queried plaintively
“Is there room for Mary there?” the inmates of
the pew found themselves crushed beneath a
weight of at least 18 stone. The sweet voices
carolled on, “Yes, there’s room—Yes, there’s
room lor Mary there! ] And they sang the
blessed truth. All obstinacy had melted away,
and the obstructors were become meek as little
children,
A very happy Melb. family of fair means and
stage-stricken tastes recently exacted a promise
from Wallace Brownlow to spend a Sunday even-
ing with them. Their directions to the domestic
staff on that occasion were : “After Mr. Brownlow
arrives, we are not at home to anybody.”
The Chirnsides announce their intention of dis-
continuing the every Wednesday meet, on their
land about the Werribee (Vic.), when Gov. Hope-
toun goes away—they are kept up solely out of
friendliness for him. He delights in live game all
through; and the Chirnsides’ paddocks are well-
stocked with deer. These small Wednesday fields
are found to be far more enjoyable than the big
Saturday affairs, which always mean a drag over
half the country to be ridden, with a fox at the
end and a steeplechase thrown in.
The two women at present awaiting their trial
for murder, in Melbourne, are of widely different
dispositions. One is hoity-toity and hard to
please. She turns up her nose at the prison fare,
and says that she wouldn’t give her dog such food,
and that it’s a shame to infeult a lady with offal.
Her companion in distress feels her position
keenly, cries most of the time, and is humbly
grateful for every kindness.
A dame of the fallen Boomocracy betook herself,
in the thick of her troubles, to a suburb where
she “knew” no one. When the . clan was
struggling in deep waters, the neighbours called
on Iher to a woman, but were not admitted. Later,
when the crowning, catastrophe was. averted, she
returned all their visits, saying that those “ who
had wept wiffh her when 'she wept must now
rejoice when she rejoiced ! ” The distinguished
emigree still retains the “ gushing ” demeanour so
fashionable in “ dear Lady Loch’s ” time, and un-
bends to any extent m her happier moments.
Recently she announced oracularly over the after-
noon tea and cake, that a certain financial
institution would soon be on its legs
again, not yielding the profits of yore but
removing all just cause of complaint. "Another
interesting revelation was the identity of the fairy
godmother who provided funds for a notoriously
ill-timed health-trip. This came rather as a sur
prise, every one knowing that in the past the
magnanimous donor did not relish the assumption
that one title is as good as another if they both
sound the same. But it’s easy to forgive a
humiliated rival, especially when one doesn’t know
what the future may have in store for all of us.
The story of the vice-regal dame who encoun-
tered her erring brother in an orderly’s uniform,
recalls the prodigal relative, discovered long ago in
Melbourne by a neighbouring Governor. The
ne’er-do-well was living on husks at the time,
having severed his connection with the legal firm
which had employed him as a copying clerk. One
■of the partners was on the committee of a “ society
for utilising educated paupers,” and through its
agency had obtained the services of His Ex.’s
relative on such terms as to make him a cheap
investment, despite his frequent alcoholisms.
Moreover, the poor fellow wasn’t lazy, though he
groaned at every stroke of his pen like a galley-
slave at the oar, and upset the decorum of the
•office by his audible and thirsty reveries. “ Satur-
day there’ll be 12s. 6d 1” he would observe, “and
then (imitating the action of drinking) gollv—
gollier—gollieat!”
No one was more surprised than the Brand
himself when he was suddenly snatched from the
Burning and transplanted to gilded quarters and
the cushioned ease of a private secretary. Alas !
he didn’t live long after his promotion,, though it
wasn’t joy that killed him. To the last he was as
open-handed as when in his worst straits he sold
matches around Melb. In tluse times, I’m told,
he often said to a casual acquaintance, “Sorry I
can’t join you in a drink, old man ! But take
three boxes of matches and negotiate a glass for
yourself.” He was hopelessly out of touch with
the self-made person who started ufe on twopence.
Gov. Hopetoun’s good-looking Private Secre-
tary, in addition to his other duties, acts as
mentor to his patron s youthful son and heir.
T-his urchin, like his mother, seems to lose his
presence of mind before the gaze of the multitude,
and on his rare public appearances has to be
prompted to take off hit cop, and furtively nudged
when the ococaslori demands that he sho dd
wreath his chubby countenance in smiles. “ Mr.
Ralston is so fond of .Lord Hope !” is a remai k
one hears very often. As most people grow more
horrid every year of their lives, no doubt young
Hopeful is a good deal less riley now than he is
likely to be 20 years hence.

Herbert Goddard, known to fame as Big Joe’s
brother, has died of consumption, and Slavin’s brother
Bill has suffered defeat. For dearth of news about the
great men the papers chronicle the doings of their
brothers.
♦ * ♦ *
Yankeeland holds a woman reporter whose
specialty is to describe boxers and boxing. One Aus-
tralian “ authority ” on pugilism is said to be regularly
trounced by a woman whose practical skill is too much for
his scientific knowledge—on paper.
* * ♦ *
That straight old sportsman, William Gannon
died at his residence, Betty’s Hotel, Sydney, on Ist inst/,
after a long illness, the root of which might perhaps be
found in the fact that his racehorses persisted in running
second.
* * #
The proposed appointment to N.S. Wales
Legislative Council of a well-known turfite (said to have
been recommended by Toby Barton of whom he is an
enthusiastic supporter) recalls that said turfite was first
heard of when a resident of Bathurst. For a wager of
£lO be carried upon his head an enormous pumpkin from
the Duke’s Hotel, in Howick-street, to the Melbourne
Hotel at Kelso (two miles), the conditions being ttrat he
was not to touch the pumpkin with his hands from the
time he put it upon his head until he took it off at the
end of the journey. He won the wager, and thereafter
settled in Sydney with the proceeds, where his straight-
ness and gameness have earned for him the foremost
position as a layer of odds on the Australian turf.

Another boom is starting in Melbourne—Bil-
liards.

Why should honest owners with good horse-
flesh be disallowed to win more than two or three decent
races ? Manifold s Apsley must now be at the end of his
winning tether after only two appearances at Victorian
head-quarters. His first Flemington appearance was in
the G.N. Hurdles, where he carried 9st. 91b. to victory
The following Saturday he was raised to list. 21b. ami
won. For the Caulfield Q.N., the handicapper seems to
recognise that his duty is to make certain of preventing
the horse from again scoring, and has treated him to 12st
71b. The obvious effect of this system is that every good
horse to clear rearing expenses must, after a few races be
sent to the stud. Why should this be ?

Brockwell scored 103 runs in a recent innings
for Surrey v. Yorkshire. Brockwell made fine bowling
and batting averages against the Australians in England
so he would be an interesting addition to Stoddart’s
flashy-looking team.
• * * *
A correspondent: The North Queenslander is
about the most genial, irreligious, hospitable, hearty, and
least sanctimonious individual in Australia. His Sabbath
is enlivened by cricket, football, balloon ascents, long
beers, and amorous peregrinations. The other day a
football match (on the Sawbath) took place at the Towers
before some 4000 spectators. A howl of indignation from
the parsonic fraternity rent the Northern welkin and
the godly Colonial Secretary, Tozer, promptly took ’steps
to prevent a return match on the following Sunday.
* » • »
The Essendons, the Vic. football champions,
had a victorious tour in S.A., and are, without doubt the
best 20 in Australia under Australian rules. They have
only been defeated twice in four years, but this is not
extraordinary, when one considers that the wealthiest
team can “ command ” (or purchase) the best players.
* ♦ ♦ »
Before long there will be five-shilling and half-
crown totalisators in S.A. The bookie is dead, and the
public justly demand something cheaper than half-sov
wagers through the machine.
♦ ♦ * *
The M‘Guire-Perry fight, although awarded to
Perry, owing to M'Guire’s hand failing, was a certainty
for the Melb. lad had things gone on to a finish. As it
was, the Sydneyite got on to his opponent’s jaw during a
clinch in a foul-suggesting way. Also, the tactics of
Perry before the fight re the arrangements of stake etc
were not calculated to increase his Southern popularity. ’’
• * » *
A fair crowd, partly inspired by curiosity and
partly disgusted at the one-sidedness and commercialism
of their own game, rolled up to each of the Interprovincial
Rugby matches on East Melb. Ground. The first match
was a remarkably fine exhibition of football and finished
up by Vic. winning by three points. Plante, three-
quarter back, a 13st. man, and Tough, who looked about
Bst., shared the honours for the victors, Lusk and Roberts
being the most prominent Sydneyites. The return match
was about as rough a game as was ever seen, and the
wonder is that no corpses were left on the field A
Roman oestus fight or an aboriginal clubbing contest
must have been mild and gentle exercise as compared
with this. N.S. W. turned the tables by 9 points to 5 the
laurels of their victory being slightly discounted by their
consistent efforts to waste time.

stated. But it will not be Fitzsimmons’ fault if he does
not meet Corbett.

from youthful folly, excesses, or any other cause, Guaran-
teed CURB. A great scientific discovery by anatomy.
Treatise on “Lost Manhood” posted free, sealed. To
young men contemplating marriage, to be married, to
young and old, this book is invaluable, pointing out as

to whom love is but a hideous, tormenting dream, life

* * • *
Melbourne Baseball Club proposes to send a
team to Adelaide for first week in Sept., but the matter of
expenses may induce it to alter the date to fit in with
a Cook’s excursion. The M. 0.0. does not give the Club
much financial backing.
• * • •
Of the many starting-machines now used on
N.S.W. racecourses, that patented by Tom Power, Rowley
Pickering's lieutenant on S.M. Herald, appears to gain
most approval.
Last week’s racing again proved the superiority
of the machine over the human article in the matter of
starting. The Sydney suburban courses are all giving it
a trial, and Randwick will soon be the only course on
which a straggling start will be seen, and where it will b$
necessary to fine the j'ockeys. That the “ big” club will
in the end adopt the machine is certain.
* « • •
L. Marsh won the 100 yards handicap at Lillie
Bridge, Sydney. At the present juncture, the starts
allotted the backmarkers are interesting: M'Garrigal,
4Jyds. ; L. Marsh, syds, ; J. Marsh, sJyds. ; Thorn, 6yds.;
W. Williams, 6Jyds,; while the once invincible J. Price
is Byds.
• • • *
Mr. S. H. Gollan, the Maoriland owner, has
bought the crack steeplechaser, Norton, with a view to
taking him to England.
« * « •
The Wallaroo and Pirates met on the Sydney
Cricket Ground, the attendance and play both proving
very poor. Wallaroo won by 9 points to 3. Randwick
and Wallaroo meet in the final for the Association Cup
next Saturday.
« • • •
N.S.W. Government House scored its first
victory on Saturday at Warwick Farm, when Mr. R. W.
Duff won the Welter Mile on Bodkin. Bodkin jumped
off with the lead, and never gave anything else a show.
The mare was well-ridden, with the exception that her
rider perhaps made more use of her at the finish than %
more experienced jockey would have done when winning
so easily, but Mr. Duff did quite right not to run the
chance of being caught napping by Mr. Wells on Round-
about.
« * * «
Upwards of a couple of thousand people wit*
nessed the University-Randwick football match on Sydney
Agricultural Ground on Saturday. Randwick played a
great game, winning by 19 points to 8, the feature of the
game being the tackling of the old warrior, “Taffy”
Parish, who showed himself a second White. The Uni-
versity barrackers were much in evidence early in the
game, but the drubbing their team underwent quietened
even the bargee emulators towards the finish.
* ♦ • •
So far Sam Hordern has been none too lucky as
an owner. The Gift, his purchase after her many wins in
Oxenham’s colours, has died from inflammation.
• • • *
One of the chief employments of the cableman
for some time past has been to announce that Wales*
yacht, Brilliant, has defeated the Yankee, Vigilant. The
victories or defeats of Wales’ yacht are of the smallest
possible interest to Australians, apart from the dreary
old fiction of Australian loyalty. If the Brilliant had
belonged to anybody else she would have been forgotten
long ago.
• ♦ • *
How is it that the Victorian Football Associa-
tion, while professing amateurism, permits professional
boxers to play in the clubs composing it? In all other
sports—rowing, running, &o.—a man who is a professional
in one is a professional in all. But this is an unfair rule,
and should be altered. For instance, a boat-builder is
declared a professional oarsman, though he may have
never sat in a boat in his life, and he is thus debarred
from running, boxing, or bicycling as an amateur.
* * * *
After all, why should not professionalism be
recognised in football ? It would be better to make foot-
ball a trade—an honest trade like the actor’s—than that
the present sneaking and quibbling and bamboozling
should continue.
♦ * * •
The Maoriland Gaming and Lotteries Act
Amendment Bill provides that the prizes offered in horse-
races, fights, etc., shall not be recoverable in a court of
law. However, that doesn’t much matter. You can’t
trust the average board of deacons. But the word of a
board of bookmakers is as good as their bond.
Australia’s best-known metallician found him-
self, early on the morning of a big race-meeting, playing
hazard with somebody who had already won £BOOO from
him. “ Doable or quits?” (£16,000 or nothing I) said the
winner. “No,” replied the bookmaker, “but I’ll play
you for £4ooo—make it £12,000 or £4OOO ?” The offer was
accepted, and the bookie won. He continued to win, and
presently contrived to owe his friend only £lOOO. Then
he arose and wrote a cheque for that amount, since which
time he has played no more hazard.

Rosebery, by his Ladas wins in the “ Triple
Crown ” events, pocketed £17,933.

It seems that the snuffling outcry against Rose-
bery’s turf connection proceeded from the slimy depths
of the “Nonconformist Conscience,” which is a sprited
duplicate of Jabez Balfour’s liver. G. R. Sims, in the
Referee, wants to know “ What right has a Noncon-
formist to a special Conscience? We don’t parade the
Church of England conscience, or the R.O. conscience, or
the Jewish conscience. But this self-advertising, smug,
sanctimonious, Nonconformist Conscience is perpetually
poking its nose into everybody’s business, and every-
body’s pleasure.” Unfortunately, too, it has, for some
years past, been religiously dipping its flabby paw into
everybody’s pocket. t , ,

Jim Barron, the well-behaved Sydney boxer,
now on his return to Sydney, had bad luck in the States.
He broke his hand during his fight with M‘Koy, and the
doctors tell him he will never be able to close it again.

trotters in Maoriland. Specification trotted four miles in
lOmm. 475e0., and three miles in 7min. 35seo. ; Black-
smith covered two miles in smin. 9seo. ; and Mr. " Bob"
Craig’s old Sydney mare, Yura Yum, is credited with a
mile in 2min. 27£sec. Pity it is Sydney owners have, I
by their crooked ways, smothered the sport in Sydney. ,

Griffo’s American backer is tired of the Aus- j
tralian’s Jonel Sullivan ways, and states that unless he

Wool and Produce Reports.
M‘Burney, Wallis & Co. report;—The appearance of
Western wool in our railway manifests is evidence that
another wool season is close at hand. The tone of the
local market is about equal to auctions last week. No
sales were held this week, and we have only private
transactions to base our opinion upon. Next week the
usual fortnightly auctions will take place, but wool offered
will be of the usual description for this time of year, viz.,
greasy oddments and scoured. Shearing in the West is
now progressing smoothly and rapidly, and from reports
the new clip should open up well. The admission of wool
free to America is still uncertain, but we have hopes that
it will be brought about, when an increase in prices here
must be the result. It is gratifying to know that several
of our large clips, which in past years have been shipped
direct to London, will be offered in Sydney this season.
Sydney, August 6, 1894.
Nrw Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.
report Wool. —The past week has been an exceptionally
quiet; one in the local market, but at the auctions fixed to
take place on Bth and 9th inst. some of the new clip wools
will be brought into the market, and this will assist, no
doubt, to impart a more animated tone to the trade. We
have already received into the store the first instalment
of the large Bogan clip, marked E. S. & 0. HALL over
WILLEROON, and this wool opens up in capital order,
comparing favourably with the growth of the same dis-
trict last year. Prom America, the latest news to hand
concerning the Tariff question is rather more hopeful, and
would seem to indicate the possibility of an early settle-
ment of the deadlock which has cropped up between the
Upper and Lower branches of the Legislature in this con-
nection.

PHYSICAL CULTURE.
A LEADING AUSTRALIAN PROFESSOR.
In the Cosmopolitan magazine for June, 1894,
Eugene Sandow, the modern Hercules, very truly
remarks : “ There is no easy road to longevity.
It is only by steady obedience to the laws of
health that old age may be attained, and by
judicious regimen that it may be prolonged.”
“THE WAY TO GET STRONG, AND HOW
TO REMAIN SO ” is the title of a prospectus
issued by Professor F. Loose concerning his well-
known “Physical Exercise Chart.” Professor
Loose, who is a disciple of the late Alexander
Maclaren, of Oxford University, the father of
modern gymnastics, was, for a number of years,
chief director of exercises prescribed for the
Queensland Police Force and the pupils (both boys
and girls) of the Grammar School and the State
schools in Brisbane. He is an expert gymnast,
and AN ACCOMPLISHED PROFESSOR OF
PHYSICAL CULTURE, and has closely studied
anatomy and physiology with the view of being
able to conduct the work of his classes on a scien-
tific basis. Coming, as it does, from such a high
authority on the scientific development of the
body, the following testimonial is of great value.
Professor Loose says : “ For the last twenty years
I have been following the profession of physical
culture, and during that time THOUSANDS OF
PUPILS have passed through my hands. Of
course accidents sometimes occur, even w hen the
greatest precautions are taken to avoid them.
In such oases I HAVE ALWAYS RECOM-
MENDED ST. JACOBS OIL, and I have never
had cause to regret it, for within a few days my
pupils were always ready to resume work. I am
not speaking with a limited experience. During
my residence in Queensland I never had less than
three thousand pupils under my charge-adults
and children of both sexes. I always keep a
bottle of St. Jacobs Oil in my house, and in oases
of stiffness or soreness I WOULD ASK FOR NO
BETTER REMEDY.” Professor Loose, whose
portrait appears above, is at present in Sydney,
where he contemplates settling. His address is
Kingscourt, Wigram-road, Forest Lodge.

Dear Bulletin,— By publishing H. M.
Winthrop’s article you come perilously near open-
ing a discussion of a tabooed subject—“ the
meejum of exchange.” Shall we not have to
return to the old idea of payment by produce
instead of fixed value? Meaning, that internal
wages, rent, and interest, should be based on the
ratio of a yearly Government valuation of leading
products, and rising or falling as products rise or
fall in monetary value. International exchange
or interest to be fixed by a yearly congress of
representatives from civilized nations. Interest
rises sympathetically and promptly as products
rise ; it can only be dragged down, or smashed by
a financial crisis. The idea is crudely expressed
I know; but, with a continuous fall in the
monetary value of products, only by something of
this kind can accounts be balanced and smashes
be averted.—B.

Editor Bulletin, — My maximum load for
elephant, camel, and horse are for ordinary
marches—l 2 to 16 miles a day at miles an
hour, ;i or 2 days’ halt in the week. “Drome-
dary ” can call them “ extended.” In re the form
of the camel’s spine, if “ Dromedary ” can manage
to look at the skeleton of a horse, he will find that
it’s spine is also arched as well as that of the
camel; but the camel’s possible load is a question
of legs. The beast is loaded when lying on the
ground with all his legs doubled under him.
When he gets up, he first unfolds his hind legs,
and rises to his knees. Then the whole weight of
the load is on the fore legs, and it must be raised
by the extensor muscles and tendons of those two
limbs alone, Are a camel’s fore legs strong enough
to straighten themselves under a load of ISOOlbs.
plus the animal’s own weight ?—Unt.
T. B. Browne’s “ Advertiser’s A.8.C.” is
in the very first rank of the world’s newspaper
gazetteers and advertising directories. The edition
for 1894 contains nearly 1000 pages of tabulated
information regarding the world’s Press, with
several excellent maps and much miscellaneous
information. A hasty glance shows that Sydney
Echo and other defunct journals are still adver-
tised as alive, while several Queenslanders, now
two years old and more, are not noticed. The
Australian section needs editing up to date.
This is the day of big things ; therefore the
biggest tailor in the big city of Sydney is a person
of note. John Graham, whose premises are
located at 510 George-street, is that person, and is
proud of the fact. His warehouse covers 500
sq. yds. of ground floor space alone. He keeps
five cutters always at work, and has the biggest
staff of workpeople constantly employed in the
biggest work-rooms in Sydney, under his own per-
sonal supervision. He has 1200 patterns on view in
some of the biggest shop-windows in the city,
which are twice as many as can be seen in one
window anywhere else. Also, he has a vast stock
always on hand, which he warrants to be all fast
colours, the newest designs, and the best quality
generally. His location in George-street, just
opposite the new markets, is one of the most
central in Sydney, and the fact makes him feel
good. To counterbalance the obtrusive bigness of
everything else about him, his prices are about the
smallest going, and he offers to produce a perfect-
fitting suit, which will gladden the wearer right
down to his boots, for 555. Also, having been
established for 24 years, he is a patriarch among
tailors, and hopes to continue so.


The Wild-Cat Column.
Westralia is moving England to grant her a
Mint. Westraliais produoingalmost as muchas N.S.W. did
when it got the privilege of coining sovereigns and paying
the expenses thereof. There is this difference, however.
The rate of exchange in '55, when Sydney got her Mint,
was often as high as 3 per cent, against N.S.W. The
localisation of mining stopped all that. At present, Mel-
bourne is doing all the work of coining for Westralia.
The yield in ’93 was estimated at 110,8900 z., and Mel-
bourne Mint coined 97,7170 z. of it. The gold passes
through the banks, and they charge the miner for
shipping, insurance, and agency, and, in some oases,
exchange on top. The exchange is 1 per cent. By
establishing coining as a local industry, the miners would
get'more for their gold. But at what expense? The
initial cost of a Mint at Fremantle or Perth would be
£55,000. The annual deficit thereafter would be about
£BOOO, for Westralia could not charge much more than
6d. per ounce for coining, and if her yield were 200,0000 z.
p.a., and if she got it all to coin and charged Bd. an ounce,
the revenue would be only £6666. The gross Mint ex-
penses would be £15,000 p.a. for years. But a Westralian
Mint would bring the Golden West into the bounds of
civilisation. At present, traders there, who want to avoid
the ruinous exchange instituted by the banks, have to
keep a current account on it; then the gold they buy
they ship direct to the Mint, and have the proceeds col-
lected by their agents. Thus do they chisel the banks
which, under reconstruction, are the mainstay of God’s
earth.
• • • ♦
Old James Service was not nearly so optimistic
at the last Commercial of Australia meeting as he was in
the joyous reconstruction period. Of the £7,000,090
advances of the “ old bank,” £3,000,000, he said, was
earning the full rate of interest, and the other £4,000,000
“was by no means lost.” This is eminently probable,
but even the possibility that a decent percentage of
£4,000,000 may be lost is a hair-raising contingency. But
the saddest official announcement which has yet been
made about the 0.8. of A. was Service’s lachrymose
statement that “ there was no conceivable circumstance
to prevent the depositors and creditors being paid in full
if the shareholders only acted together.” The inference
is that if the shareholders don’t act together the creditors
and depositors may not be paid. And there is nothing
said about the shareholders coming out all right whether
they act together or not.
* • • *
The “ Australasian Rights ” Bill people—who
seek to grab and monopolise at public expense the water-
power of the Colo and Grose Valley, the only natural force
available for the cheap production of electric power for
the lighting of Sydney and its environs—talk, in their less
responsible moments, of ultimately clearing £200,000 a
year by the grab. As there are no railway jobs going
now, some of the big railway-contracting syndicates are
in full cry after this magnificent hunk of plunder, con-
cerning the possibilities of which the public are still per-
fectly ignorant. The gang reckons that it holds the
present N.S.W. Assembly in the hollow of its hand, and
rejoices exceedingly in the defeat at the polls of many
opponents of this splendid prospective robbery—which
by the way, has its parallels in Melbourne and Tasmania,
where similar, though less extensive, grabs are in progress.

Goldsbrough, Morfc and Co., Limited, has
declared, by a special clause in the articles of association,
that the proceeds of all goods sold on behalf of customers
shall, in future, be paid into a separate trust-account, and
kept entirely apart from the firm’s own bank-account.
This wise practice has been followed for some time past
but it is now made compulsory for the first time. ’
• • • «
That blighted monopoly, the Melbourne Gas
Co., Just manages to scrape up a dividend at the rate of
10 per cent, per annum—a serious falling-away from the
beautiful 14 per cent, of only a year ago. Considering
the difficulty with which the said div, has been raised,
the steady decline in consumption, the constant spread
of electricity, and the obvious fact that even a gas
employe’s wages can’t be reduced below nothing, it isn’t
likely that the good old days will ever come back. In
fact, the 10 per cent, div., as likely as not, will presently
drop to eight or thereabouts.

Vio. Treasurer Carter, who has all along resisted
the spieler-like tactics of Melb. Gag Co. in the City
Council, now astonishes everybody by including a 10 per
cent, kerosene duty in his Budget. What does this mean?
Melb. is just beginning to throw off the Gas Co. incubus
which has been sitting on its chest for years, and now the
implacable enemy of the said bloodsucking institution
chips in to help bis weakening antagonist.

Premier Patterson pointed out, in dealing with
Labour member Winter’s Bill for the abolition of distress
for rent, that “ it might lead to landlords demanding rent
in advance." But the timber-trade in Melbourne is only
kept moving by the demand for “To Let ” hoardings.
• • • «
Vio. Government was lately given a good chance
of showing its confidence in the galvanised banks. A
number of M.sP. interviewed the Premier to ask him to
take bank deposit receipts at their face value in payment
of interest owing by water-trusts. Patterson fairly
exploded at the idea. The strength of his faith in the
bunged institutions is further shown by the fact that this
was the only way of getting anything at all out of said
water-trusts.
• • • e
AfcQ.N. Bank annual meeting, the chairman
regretfully observed that “ the bank, like other institu-
tions in Australia, is weighted by the amount of interest
it has to pay depositors.” The Q.N. has evidently a per-
sonal grievance against those callous depositors, who
insist on slicing their £ of flesh from its palpitating
vitals. There can be no doubt that depositors would have
no place whatever in a well-considered scheme of bank
re-creation, and when they are hard-hearted enough to
refuse to do the happy despatch themselves, the banks
have a right to do it for them.
* * * *
The person chased by the last-issued Melb.
warrant has betted heavily, but neither his friends nor
the Vic. bookies can believe he lost money at the game.
He was the most consistently lucky winner on both
ponies and horses in Melbourne. About three weeks ago,
owing to a misunderstanding, his winnings on one race
were all put on the second race, and when that came off
put on the third. He only knew about the mistake when
the bookie who was struck for the “ accumulator ” came
up to him, after the third event had come off successfully,
and said he wanted time to pay at least £3OO of the
amount.

Melbournian : “ Circumstances have brought
under my notice the popular ignorance of law. Nine-
tenths of the people who mortgage imagine that the
security covers all the liability, simply because mortgages
are, as a rule, so much below value. Consequently, the
question has not arisen, till a period of awful depreciation,
as now. The common thing now is to see the Chapelly
association, of the Building Society Commytee, having
devoured the security, going beyond, and torturing the
victim under a wine press screw. Only once in a while is
there such a revenge as seeing the Y.M.C.A. in the hands
of the mortgagees. So M’Neill’s most gluttonous
auditors, in his denunciation of the churches, are the
Building Society victims.”

• ♦ ♦ «
Premier Seddon has a bill before Maoriland
Legislature by which no pawnbroker is to be allowed to
charge more than 20 percent, peran. interest on loans. This
is another interference with what the Sydney Telegraph
calls “ the natural rate of interest.” According to that
paper, any attempt to create “ artificial ” cheapness in
money only means that “the difference between the
natural and the artificial rate will come out of the pockets
of the community." Query : When the Maoriland Moses
has to accept 20 per cent, interest instead of 100, how will
he get at the pockets of the community for the difference ?

The N.S.W. bankruptcy laws permit a man to
file his schedule in forma pauperis, but when he presents
himself to do so some 30s. in fees are demanded.
• * • *
One of Australia’s best-known and erstwhile
most opulent usurers has fallen on bad times, compara-
tively, for he still does a little bit of money-lending on the
quiet. When nearly a millionaire, his avarice over-
reached itself, and now when deploring his ill-luck he
will tell you that he “ veeps one hour every night ” before
retiring to his usurious couch.
* • * *
An enormous consignment of British whisky,
at fourteen pence per gallon, including freight and
charges, was bonded in Melbourne last vear. No more
shocking evidence could be offered as to the present
quality of the local-made article. The importer pays cost
of shipment and 9d. a quart in extra dutv-tax upon this
cheap foreign poison, and still he finds it better value
than the stuff he can buy upon the spot. In other words,
a Victorian distiller can’t supply a whisky of similar smell
and flavour at nearly treble the price. There is no ques-
tion of special brand or Hinglish prejudice in a case like
this. The liquor is bought on its merits, and a fortune
sticks out for any Victorian firm that can imitate the
aforesaid fourteenpenny .(say a shilling, shippers’ price)
goods at two shillings.
* • * *
Typical Melbourne case. A merchant, with a
bank-overdraft running into five figures, got a letter from
the bank-manager, asking him to call round. He called.
“What do you intend doing?” “I intend issuing
cheques as usual. What do you intend doing ? ” “ Oh,
we can’t pay them; you must reduce your overdraft.”
“All serene. If you can’t pay them, I must go insol-
vent." “ For Heaven’s sake, you must not do that! We
must try and pull you along,hut you must try and reduce
the amount." Only sound men get bank-crushed these
times.
• • * •
Half the old Zeehan and Dundas crowd, who
laid on much fat during the silver boom, are mostly dead-
beats at Coolgardie. One well-known lawyer is working
on shift at Bayley’s Reward, and a lucky scrip-dealer
wields an auctioneer’s hammer with fair success; many of
the rest moon around the empty bars looking for nuggets.
A Grenfell (N.S.W.) correspondent sends some
most convincing samples of granitoid-looking quartz,
carrying heavy gold, from the local “ Young O’Brien"
claim, where, he says, Fitch and party are now sinking a
new shaft, Bft. x 4ft., to facilitate working. The reef is
showing 180 ft. in length, both at the roof of drive and
bottom—payable Stone all through. Samples sent are
from 180 ft. level; reef, 4ft. They have now 100 tons at
grass, and reckon it should average 2oz. Six tons are
being sent to Clyde works for trial. The locals are satis-
fied. The deeper they get, the better the stone.
“The Homeward Bound reef” (continues our cor-
respondent)—“depth, 400ft.—wentas high as soz. perton,
and No. 1, the prospecting claim, six men’s ground,
panned out £lO,OOO per man. Last stone crushed, from
380 ft. level, went HJdwt.; reef 6ft. to 7ft. wide. 24 years
ago one crushing paid the six men £l5OO each clear div. in
nine weeks. The present holders are said to be now on
2oz. stone. With practical miners used to sinking, and a
little capital to back them, Grenfell can be backed against
all the rushes for good, steady work.’’
• * ♦ •
Says a Bulletin local correspondent:—“ The
Lucknow reef, Grenfell (N.S.W.), the prospecting claim of
which yielded up to soz. to the ton (six men cleared out
with £9OOO each), should, in this time of gold-mine re-
vival, afford a fair show for men of pluck and dollars.
The Golden Point reef, Grenfell (depth, 150 ft., last lot
of stone taken out 20 years ago, ground sound), aban-
doned on account of very wet weather and the Gulgong
rush being on, yielded as high as 7oz. per ton, and should
be well worth while looking after.”

The whispered assurances that Bayley’s gold
didn’t go down were falsified last week when some of the
richest stone yet met was got in sinking the shaft below

colossal reef, which carries coarse gold all through. Cos-
grove’s Bayley’s South, which was withdrawn from
auction by the sheriff, the other day, has, it is said,
also struck wonderfully rich gold at 80ft.

500 oz.

The Londonderry prospectors, at latest, had a
quarter of a ton of gold in specimens at grass, and had
not decided whether to dolly it, or keep as specimens. If
they are going to float their mine they should hang on to
their specimens, than which nothing richer or more allur-
ing has yet been seen. Three or four tons of Londonderry
specimens would raise the roof off London, if we may
Judge from the furore excited by a oouple of pounda of
stone exhibited in George-street, Sydney.
The quartz is glittering, sugary, crystalline stuff—the
sort of “ tack " that the children of Israel probably saw
in their dreams of Mount Hoaxley.

There are rumours in London that there is a
hitch in the negotiations respecting the Great Boulder
mine, Westralia, which Zeb. Lane has got Colonel North
into. In doing this Zeb. performed a tour de force , for the
Nitrate King has hitherto fought shy of gold-mines. By
the way, North recently made nearly £BOO,OOO in one
Nitrate property deal, says a Bullbtin correspondent.
The name of Westralia, says Thk Bulletin’s
London correspondent, is still insufficient to charm cash
out of the hard-hit English investor; even parsons have
ceased to be_ allured by cleverly-worded prospectuses.
The Coolgardie Co. cannot go to allotment, as some of
the directors know what they are about and refused to go
on with insufficient capital. It is said now that the capital
Of Mawson’s was not fully subscribed, although the city
liars industriously spread the story that it had been over-
subscribed. Such is the state of the mining market that
I don’t think even Mount Morgan would float for a million.
Promoters killed the goose that laid the golden egg.
Next year Westralia will probably exceed Bal-
larat district’s yield for 1893 207,8170 z.
♦ * * ♦
_ Westralia’s actual gold-results, so far, are really
trifling, and it is only its mortgage on the future that
justifies gush. Knock two millions of ounces off Victoria’s
palmiest annual yields and the odd hundred thousands
beat Westralia’s present performance with ridiculous
ease. The last shipment landed in Melbourne included
the cream of the Londonderry, and all told it barely beat
a normal Bendigo or Ballarat yield for a fortnight.
H. W. Cheesbrough writes from Coolgardie,
June 15“ Let me give you some idea of what a small
local rush is like. Yesterday a man came in from eight
miles out, and showing some 7oz. of alluvial, applied for
a Reward Claim. His name was Bonnie, a very quiet,
decent fellow, and there is no doubt that he got the gold
where he indicated. Many put a coil on bluey and cleared
out straight away. After tea, excitement raged.
The bellman patrolled Bayley-street, and, with leathern
lungs, wildly announced the fact that ‘time was now on
the wing. Roll up, roll up! Teams are now leaving for the
new rush at the 8-mile.’ In ten minutes, out of high-
way and by-way, they did ‘roll up.’ Some carried their
tools and swags, others stacked their belongings into
carts which quickly appeared on the scene. And off they
went, 200 of them, straight away. They were followed
by a continuous stream of others, who poured on to the
new field all through the night. Other teams followed in
the morning. The country for miles round Bonnie’s
claim gleamed with camp-fires and bristled with pegs—
from the modest deal-peg, split off a box and carried
out, to a forest-king of hacked-about gimlet-wood.
About midnight some humorous individual announced in
an excited manner that ‘ there was another rush about
two miles South.’ A mob packed up and cleared out at
a sling-trot without more ado. All night until daylight
men argued and cursed and got up and walked about over
each other s legs. Next morning, after a hasty meal, the
country was tickled and scratched in every direction for
miles, but it responded not. Before midday there was a
pretty general cessation of work, and all hands inter-
viewed the prospector, to look at the gold. This
took a few minutes. Then there was another rush 100
yards to the eastward. Hundreds of frantic men crowded
round the spot and it was ascertained that one lucky
fellow had found a small lizard, which he carefully stowed
away in a matchbox. This raised their spirits a bit.
Afterwards a numerous party took possession of the
‘ Reward Claim,’ upon which a ‘school ’ was soon busy at
‘ two-up.’ Another party of humourists stuck a whisky-
bottle in the fork of a gimlet-wood tree, and whiled away
the time hurling chunks of quartz at it. Others returned
to the prospector and threatened to work across the
‘ Reward Claim ’ 50 abreast, and if gold was found it was
to be given to him. Failing the precious metal, he was
to be ‘ slung ’ so far into tbe lower regions that a 40-horse
power telescope would be required to sight him. How-
ever, general good humour prevailed, and it all ended in
sulphurous language, the great bulk of the ‘ rushers *
coming back to the town as hungry as hunters, and as
jolly as sandboys. And that’s how we do it at Cool-
gardie.”

The Day Dawn, Murchison, has cleared up

• • • •
Editor Bulletin,—ln your issue of June 23rd,
Just to hand, you publish a letter in which the Murchison
is damned generally. Now, we have become quite
hardened to having our 3 to 15oz. per ton returns from
the New Chum, Star of East, Emerald, etc., mines pub-
lished in the Eastern papers under the heading of “Cool-
gardie Gold Returns,” and to being generally over-
shadowed by the newspaper padding of the Southern
Field, but we must now buck. Taking your correspon-
dent’s statements in order—Fever is not rife; Seven
doctors do find work, but what their earnings may be is
best known to themselves, for there is scarcely a case of
sickness on the field. Goods are not, nor have they been,
at famine prices, for during the time that I have been on
the field (six months) I have managed to live very com-
fortably for less than 20s. per week. The gold “ shoots ’’
are not “narrow” nor confined, one to a fissure, but are
much the same as on any other good reefing field in Aus-
tralia. The lodes are strong and make stronger at depth,
and the shoots extend in oases 200 ft. to 500 ft. in length.
Your correspondent coins a new expression to suit
his outburst, on the Murchison, by using the term
“bugholes ” in stone, and it can be very easily
seen that his whole knowledge of mining and of the
field generally is made up of pure hearsay, and that,
having heard of “vughs” and “vugholes,” he has
thought fit to alter the accepted rendering to the not too
sweet appellation of “bugholes.” The stone is entirely
the opposite to that described by your correspondent,
and is a very free, “sugary,” laminated variety, between
beautifully clean walls ; “slippery heads” are the usual
thing, and the gold is well distributed throughout the
body. This is the rule, the exception is to find rich
patches in “ buck ” quartz, and probably with a propor-
tion in the “ vughs,” but the statement that “ the know-
ing ones can collect nearly all the gold in shoots by
cleaning out the ‘bugs’ and then dollying” is as
utterly absurd as it is untrue. There are 15 to 20
different mines down to water-level, and there is
not one instance of a lode having pinched or deteriorated,
but 75 per cent, of them have more settled, larger
bodies with better gold. The fissure lodes are true and
persistent. Instances: Leviathan line, 1J miles long, 4ft.
to 6ft. wide; Light of Asia, 1J miles long, 10ft. to 14ft.
wide; Campagna, 1 mile long, 2ft. to 3ft. wide; Sailor
Belt, probably 2 miles long, 4ft. to Bft. wide ; Day Dawn,
6ft. to 18ft. wide over 1 mile ; and all proved to over 100 ft.
in depth, and payable.
The mining regulations are not lax, they are as
strictly enforced as the Warden can fix them, but owing
to the defective W.A. Mining Act there are many loop-
holes. The maliciously incorrect statement about crush-
ing returns “ running from 3dwt. to 4oz. 7dwt., and being
picked at that” is effectually rebutted by the returns
from the mills as under. In conclusion, in my opinion
the field requires no booming nor newspaper padding to
set it going, but when it receives a “ foul blow,” like your
correspondent’s letter, it simply makes us rise up like one
man and fire off the truth to you. What we do want are
batteries, and there is stone enough at grass to keep 50
heads going for two or three years. We are quite content
to have the field as it is, and don’t want to be boomed and
rushed “yet a while,” and the tale of the Murchison will
be told in the most fitting way in the near future by
ounces and dividends. Returns to date are
tons ozs. dwts. ozs. dwts.

( Dear Bulletin, —Print and count out the words,
“ Wyalong proper squats on an unhealthy flat.” Seven,
ain’t it? Taking No.’s three and four as one verb, there
can only be one word of it true: that is No. 2. Wyalong
proper is Wyalong West, where the business, mining,
amusements and proper persons (including myself) are.
It doesn’t squat. It moves longitudinally and latitu-
dinally day-by-day. It is not unhealthy, and it is not
flat. Otherwise the statement is correct, and either lor
your other correspondent is “cronk."—Yours, Scotds
or W., Wyalong West, Aug, 4th, 1894.

ex-Premier Dibbs bears his sudden reverse of
fortune. But yesterday he might have stood
against the world, and to-day ha does stand
against it. There is not a feather ruffled in his
knightly coat; not the reflection of the shadow of
the likeness of the sign of the ghost of a droop to
be seen in his ex-Republioan crest. Though a
shattered political world has fallen in fragments
around him, the ruins strike him fearless—as
Horace beautifully puts it. Would it be treason
to hint that what gives strength to his mind, elas-
ticity to his step, and colour to his complexion, is

When the Reid Ministry was being formed
the S.M. Herald sent a reporter to interview Mr.
H. Parkes, and Parkes told him—according to
the printed account of the interview—that he
would never take hoffice again. Then the old
man repented of the statement, and wrote to the
Herald to flatly deny that he had over made any
suoh hobservafcion. Consequently, either the
Herald reporter or Parkes is lying, and, as the
Herald doesn’t make the slightest attempt to
vindicate its own employee, it would seem that it
is quite willing he should be the liar rather than
that Parkes should be put in an awkward situation.
The average paper isn’t built that way. When a
wild-eyed politician makes reckless statements to
its reporter, and then wants to go back on them,
the average paper responds with a brick.
A sheep is a conscientious animal, and if
he gets a fair chance he will try hard to do credit
to his pastures and temporal masters. At an in-
credibly early age a sheep measures himself with
the universe, and calculates how best to apportion
the 24 hours so as to get the greatest amount of
wool on. Generally, he decides to devote 16 hours
to feed, eight to sleep, and all to fatten. But it’s
no use a sheep making good resolutions if his
owner won’t spend a halfpenny on dip to save
him from ticks and vermin. There are no flies on
a sheep sentimentally, but plenty trouble him
practically. How is it possible for a sheep to do
himself justice and grow smooth, long, lustrous
staple when he has to continually chase the root
of his tail in fruitless endeavour to bite the ticks
snugly planted there. It is nothing to the pur-
pose to say that the situation is productive of
Romeo and Juliet romance between a gentleman
tick and a lady sheep, or vice versa. Woolgrowers
don’t want romance these times ; they want wool
to reduce the overdraft with, and when a tick and
a sheep are crossed in love (as they alwavs are
crossed) the tender sheep pines away and frets ;
he gets thin and mournful, and all the yolk goes out
of his wool. On the contrary, if a sheep is well
dipped ho has no bad dreams, and grows as fat
and jolly as an alligator, while his wool is worth
twice as much as that of the weary animal who
has to wake up every five minutes and ask his
brother to scratch him. Now, Cooper’s Dipping
Powder, for which Harrison, Jones, and Devlin
are agents, is about the most effective in the
market, as is shown by the certified fact that the
sale increased by 1,140,170 packets last year. At
last Melbourne Sheep Show sheep which had been
dipped with Cooper secured 30 prizes. Sensible
sheep-owners who see these things will surely
ponder and dipper and Oooper.

The Missing Lady.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Ho seemed a Melbourne hunemployed, and
anxiously his eye
Shot glances up and down the street at all the
passers-by; .
From weary hour to weary hour he shifted not hxs
place, ,
He stared with awful interest at every woman e
face,
I saw him when the sun shone high above his hat-
less head ;
I saw him when it sank to rest in western glory
red; ... ,
And in the stormy early morn I felt a thrill of
pain
On finding him still watching there, and getting
wet in vain.
“ My friend,” I said, “the air is cold, you’re out
of luck, I fear : ,
Accept the wherewithal to buy a pint or two of
beer.” . ..
He took the proffered silver coin, and spat on it to
prove . ,
He bore no animosity—but yet he didn t move.
“My silent friend, this doorstep’s mine ; bleak
wintry breezes blow ; '
You must be chilly sitting here, it’s really time
Then, looking north and looking south, that
shabby stranger man ‘ .
Said, “ Don’t disturb a honest bloke wot s workm
all he can.”
“ I’ve got a job, and that’s a fact; I’m workin
here on spec.” .
(The kindly raindrops washed a track adown his
grimy neck).
“ Yersee, there’ll be some more reward for anyone
to win ,
Who spots that missing lady when she wanders
off agin.
“ Her folks ’ave paid to get ’er back a dozen
times, or more,
And if she’s there , or if she ain’t, yer never know
for sure. , ,
I mean to catch ’er on the ’op, and see er sate
restor’d
When next’ they advertise ’er with a ‘liberal
reward. ’
“ I’ve learned ’er ‘ full description ’ well, I know
’er age and weight, .
She’s bound to cross my bloomin’ path, so elp me I
soon or late;
And while the times is what they are, and other
trades is slack,
I mean to wait and seize my chance for takin of
’er back.”
So, like the raven, gaunt and grim, immortalised
by Poe, ,
My friend sits on the doorstep, whilst I daily
come and go
(He is also like the lilies which do toil not, neither
spin), .1*
Watching ever for the lady who will soon be off
agin.
Too many cooks spoil the policeman.
£ £ Smith, the pertinacious Melb. apothe-

selves.
Dear Bulletin,— The bullock - driver is
rapidly becoming extinct, and it’s a pity. He was
the only genuine whole-souled type of man we
ever had in Australia. His oaths had the true
ring of the bush, and smelt of gum-leaves and
wattle, also of “Tartarean sulphur and strange
fire.” He never swore except at his bullocks or a
new-chum, and three of his curses went further
than 40 from anyone else, either with men or
bullocks. He got drunk, too, did this bullock-
driver sometimes, and then he made things
shift. He got the horrors sometimes, too, and
ran out naked, on all fours, to round
up his bullocks, barking like a dog, and he
has even been known when one bullock was miss-
ing to yoke himself up with the near-side poler
and, whip in hand, start off on a day’s stage ; bub
this didn’t occur often and didn’t last long, for on
such occasions it ended abruptly by a “trap’
yoking himself up with the bullocky and striking
a bee-line for the nearest police-station. Next to
cursing the bullocky was an artist with his fists.
He could and would fight, and he fought with or
without cause, whichever came first, with fre-
quency and fury. Well I mind the time two bul-
lockies met at a camping-ground at the foot of the
Moonbies at an old hut. Both unyoked together,
and both wanted to bake. Both got gum-bark
and put it on the fire together, and eight or nine
bullockies looked on in silence and suspense.
Each emptied out his flour and began to mix it
into a damper ; then both reached for the sjhovel
together, and the storm burst. Outside they went
and battered each other for 80 minutes and paused
for breath. Said one : “This won t get my
damper into the fire,” and began to pull his shirt
over his battered and blood-stained head. Ihe
other said nothing, and all went back to the hut,
and the dampers were cooked in silence. Yours,
H. J. Mac. [An improvement on the snake-
perjuries.—Ed. B.]

PROSPECTUS OjET" THE
Oapitaine Oil Engine Co.,
LIMITED.
(To be incorporated under the Companies Act, 37 Vic., No. 19, as a Limited Liability Co.)
CAPITAL— £SOOO, in 100 Shares of £5O each (with power to increase); 25 Shares to be
issued as fully paid-up, and 50 Shares of the balance are offered to the public on
the following terms, viz. :
£l2 10s. per Share on application and £l2 10s. per Share on allot-
ment f and the balance in calls of not more than £3 per Share, at
intervals of not less than one month.
(For the present it is not proposed to call up more than ,£25 per Share).
THIS Company is formed for the purpose of acquiring the Stock, Goodwill, Agency, and Patent
Colon y of New South Wales of “The Capitaine Kerosene Oil Engine.” It is
oeneved that the future prospects of this engine are such as completely warrant the formation of the
proposed Company.
• -3^ r th . e - P ur P°? e of showing the advantages of this engine a perusal of the following particulars is
invited, which, it is submitted, make at once apparent the suitability of the engine to the exigencies
or colonial requirements : 6
They are worked with ordinary Kerosene.- No bulky fuel.
They are worked at low cost.
They will work anywhere.
They are light in weight (about one-third of a steam engine).
Ihey are compact (about one-fourth the size of a steam engine).
They are ready for work in five minutes.—No waiting for getting up steam.
are without any danger whatever.—No fear of explosions,
ihey have no boiler; therefore no boiler inspections, and no troubles attendant to same.
Ihey require no attendance whatever while in motion, except lubricating once about every hour.
The require no increase of fire insurance.
They are perfectly clean. —No coal, no ashes; no splashing of oil.
They are portable.
They do not require a skilled engineer.
They run smoothly and evenly.
They work nearly silent.
They save fuel automatically if not performing their full work.
Ihey are independent of gas, coal, wood, and water supply.

“ This life insurance idea is very lovely,’’
said the big capitalist, “if a man only dies young,
but suppose I should live 30 or 40 years longer,
why I’ll be terribly out of pocket.’’ “ Don’t worry
yourself about that,” replied the agent; “you’ll
soon kill yourself working to pay the premium.”—

weekly, supporting Gov. Hopetoun in his belief
that “ we were born to be happy ”
A little boy who has just been born at Downshire
House, in Belgrave Square, is the heir to £96,000 a year,
large estates in County Down and Berkshire, a very fine
London house in the very centre of the fashionable
quarter, a tnarquisate, two earldoms (one Irish and one
English), two Irish and one English viscounty, an English
and an Irish barony.
Praise God from whom all blessings flow!
THE PILOT.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Trouble has again arisen through a certain clergyman
refusing to attend a pauper funeral. This is, apparently,
a revival of the trouble of 1892. —North Queensland
paper.
Said the pilot to the pilot: “Should a sinner
chance to die
And require a Christian burial as he seeks his
Home on high,
Let him first pay to the pilot all the dues he can
afford,
Let him grease the fatted carcase—for the glory
of the Lord !”
Said the pilot to the pilot; “ Let us make a com
pact sure,
Let us organise and make a bond that ever shall
endure;
Let us stipulate for carriages and wine and festive
board
Ere we do our sacred duties—for the glory of the
Lord !”
Said the pilot to the pilot: “It is really too
absurd
To think the shepherd goes afoot to see the sheep
interred ;
‘ As thy purse so shall thy days be ’ is a rule that’s
now ignored.
But, please Heaven, we’ll see it followed—for the
glory of the Lord 1”
Then the pilots swore a dreadful oath—by Peter’s
holy pate,
And by the sacred stipend and by the Gulden
Gate,
They would blast the poor defaulter witli un-
animous accord—
They would boycott him to Hades—for the glorv
of the Lord !
So, come hither with the carriage, and come hither
with the wine,
So, come hither with the dainties and let the pilot
dine;
When you’re on your last long journey and the
country’s unexplored,
Pray be careful, for your own sake—and the glory
of the Lord 1

The Dead Infant.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
All silent is the room,
There is no stir of breath.
Save mine, as in the gloom
I sit alone with Death.
Short life it had, the sweet,
Small babe here lying dead,
With tapers at its feet
And tapers at its head.
Dear little hands, too frail
Their grasp on Life to hold ;
Dear little mouth so pale,
So solemn, and so cold ;
Small feet that nevermore
About the house shall run ;
Thy little life is o’er !
Thy little journey done !
Sweet infant, dead too soon,
Thou shalt no more behold
The face of sun or moon,
Or starlight clear and cold.
Nor know, where thou art gone,
The mournfulness and mirth
We know who dwell upon
This, sad, glad, mad old earth.
The foolish hopes and fond
That cheat us to the last
Thou shalt not feel; beyond
All these things thou hast passed.
The struggles that upraise
The soul by slow degrees
To God through weary days—
Thou hast no part in these.
And at thy childish play
Shall we, 0 little one,
No more behold thee ? Nay,
No more beneath the sun.
Death’s sword may well be bared
’Gainst those grown old in si l ife,
But, ah ! it might have spared
Thy little unlived life.
Why talk as in despair ?
Just God, whose rod I kiss.
Did not make thee so fair
To end thy life at this.
There is some pleasant shore—
Far from His Heaven of Pride,
Where those strong souls who bore
His Cross in bliss abide—
Some place where feeble things,
For life’s long war too weak,
Young birds with unfledged wings,
Buds nipped by storm-winds bleak ;
Young lambs left all forlorn
Beneath a bitter sky ;
Meek souls to sorrow born,
Find refuge when they die.
There day is one long dawn,
And from the cups of flowers
Light dew-filled clouds updrawn,
Rain soft and perfumed showers.
Child Jesus walketh there,
Amidst child-angel bands,
With smiling lips, and fair
White roses in his hands.
I kiss thee on the brow,
I kiss thee on the eyes—
Farewell! Thy home is now
The Children’s Paradise.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
“Five-Skull-Island,"
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was in Batavia I met him—at the Hotel
des Indes—the leather-visaged, stormy-voiced
P|in of a very powerful man. I shall call him
Thurston. He laughed when I spoke of Five-
bkull-lsland.
‘‘Where they found the five skeletons?” he
said. “ I see you’ve made the Aru voyage. Or
was it the Ke? Lived in Ke, once, myself! ”
“Yes ! Would he take anything ? ”
He would, and did—then did again, and ex-
panded.
“ You’re a young chap to have gone poking
down into that out-of-the-way corner. Not
trade, I can see ! Money to throw away, eh ?
Or is it science? Never mind! About this
I ive-Skuli business ! People here will tell you
i m the man that sailed a prahu single-handed
from Ke to Celebes. Well—l didn’t ! Six of us
if, ~the others stopped half-way —at Five-
fekull-Island. Don’t care if I tell you ! ”
And here—a little dramatised—is what he did
tell me.
• * •
“The wind at last !” said Thurston, and
sat up. “Take!”
wasn ’t beside him. The edge of dawn
made that clear enough, and the white man got
